# Simple but Powerful Pratt Parsing from Matklad
<details open="true">
<summary>Expand to view code</summary>
<pre className="cire"><code>
<span className="keyword">{`use`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ std`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`::`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`{`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`fmt`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`,`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ io`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`::`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">std::io
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub trait BufRead
where
    Self: Read,
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>A <code>BufRead</code> is a type of <code>Read</code>er which has an internal buffer, allowing it<br>
to perform extra ways of reading.</p>
<p>For example, reading line-by-line is inefficient without using a buffer, so<br>
if you want to read by line, you'll need <code>BufRead</code>, which includes a<br>
[<code>read_line</code>] method as well as a [<code>lines</code>] iterator.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<p>A locked standard input implements <code>BufRead</code>:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::io;
use std::io::prelude::*;

let stdin = io::stdin();
for line in stdin.lock().lines() {
    println!(&quot;{}&quot;, line?);
}
</code></pre>
<p>If you have something that implements <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/trait.Read.html"><code>Read</code></a>, you can use the <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/buffered/bufreader/struct.BufReader.html"><code>BufReader</code><br>
type</a> to turn it into a <code>BufRead</code>.</p>
<p>For example, [<code>File</code>] implements <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/trait.Read.html"><code>Read</code></a>, but not <code>BufRead</code>.<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/buffered/bufreader/struct.BufReader.html"><code>BufReader</code></a> to the rescue!</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::io::{self, BufReader};
use std::io::prelude::*;
use std::fs::File;

fn main() -&gt; io::Result&lt;()&gt; {
    let f = File::open(&quot;foo.txt&quot;)?;
    let f = BufReader::new(f);

    for line in f.lines() {
        let line = line?;
        println!(&quot;{line}&quot;);
    }

    Ok(())
}
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="constructor">{`BufRead`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`}`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`;`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`

`}</span><span className="keyword">{`enum`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><span id="def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_11_5" className="type">{`S`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`{`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
\ \ \ \ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs::S
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">Atom(char)
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>size = 12 (0xC), align = 0x4, no Drop</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span id="def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_12_4" className="constructor">{`Atom`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">char
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>A character type.</p>
<p>The <code>char</code> type represents a single character. More specifically, since<br>
'character' isn't a well-defined concept in Unicode, <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode<br>
scalar value</a>'.</p>
<p>This documentation describes a number of methods and trait implementations on the<br>
<code>char</code> type. For technical reasons, there is additional, separate<br>
documentation in <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/core/char/index.html">the <code>std::char</code> module</a> as well.</p>
<h1 id="validity-and-layout">Validity and Layout</h1>
<p>A <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode scalar value</a>', which is any '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#code_point">Unicode code point</a>'<br>
other than a <a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#surrogate_code_point">surrogate code point</a>. This has a fixed numerical definition:<br>
code points are in the range 0 to 0x10FFFF, inclusive.<br>
Surrogate code points, used by UTF-16, are in the range 0xD800 to 0xDFFF.</p>
<p>No <code>char</code> may be constructed, whether as a literal or at runtime, that is not a<br>
Unicode scalar value. Violating this rule causes undefined behavior.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Each of these is a compiler error
['\\u{D800}', '\\u{DFFF}', '\\u{110000}'];
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Panics; from_u32 returns None.
char::from_u32(0xDE01).unwrap();
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Undefined behavior
let _ = unsafe { char::from_u32_unchecked(0x110000) };
</code></pre>
<p>Unicode scalar values are also the exact set of values that may be encoded in UTF-8. Because<br>
<code>char</code> values are Unicode scalar values and functions may assume <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/core/primitive.str.html#invariant">incoming <code>str</code> values are<br>
valid UTF-8</a>, it is safe to store any <code>char</code> in a <code>str</code> or read<br>
any character from a <code>str</code> as a <code>char</code>.</p>
<p>The gap in valid <code>char</code> values is understood by the compiler, so in the<br>
below example the two ranges are understood to cover the whole range of<br>
possible <code>char</code> values and there is no error for a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/book/ch06-02-match.html#matches-are-exhaustive">non-exhaustive match</a>.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let c: char = 'a';
match c {
    '\\0' ..= '\\u{D7FF}' =&gt; false,
    '\\u{E000}' ..= '\\u{10FFFF}' =&gt; true,
};
</code></pre>
<p>All Unicode scalar values are valid <code>char</code> values, but not all of them represent a real<br>
character. Many Unicode scalar values are not currently assigned to a character, but may be in<br>
the future (&quot;reserved&quot;); some will never be a character (&quot;noncharacters&quot;); and some may be given<br>
different meanings by different users (&quot;private use&quot;).</p>
<p><code>char</code> is guaranteed to have the same size, alignment, and function call ABI as <code>u32</code> on all<br>
platforms.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::alloc::Layout;
assert_eq!(Layout::new::&lt;char&gt;(), Layout::new::&lt;u32&gt;());
</code></pre>
<h1 id="representation">Representation</h1>
<p><code>char</code> is always four bytes in size. This is a different representation than<br>
a given character would have as part of a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/std/string/struct.String.html"><code>String</code></a>. For example:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec!['h', 'e', 'l', 'l', 'o'];

// five elements times four bytes for each element
assert_eq!(20, v.len() * size_of::&lt;char&gt;());

let s = String::from(&quot;hello&quot;);

// five elements times one byte per element
assert_eq!(5, s.len() * size_of::&lt;u8&gt;());
</code></pre>
<p>As always, remember that a human intuition for 'character' might not map to<br>
Unicode's definitions. For example, despite looking similar, the 'é'<br>
character is one Unicode code point while 'é' is two Unicode code points:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut chars = &quot;é&quot;.chars();
// U+00e9: 'latin small letter e with acute'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{00e9}'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(None, chars.next());

let mut chars = &quot;é&quot;.chars();
// U+0065: 'latin small letter e'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0065}'), chars.next());
// U+0301: 'combining acute accent'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0301}'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(None, chars.next());
</code></pre>
<p>This means that the contents of the first string above <em>will</em> fit into a<br>
<code>char</code> while the contents of the second string <em>will not</em>. Trying to create<br>
a <code>char</code> literal with the contents of the second string gives an error:</p>
<pre><code class="language-text">error: character literal may only contain one codepoint: 'é'
let c = 'é';
        ^^^
</code></pre>
<p>Another implication of the 4-byte fixed size of a <code>char</code> is that<br>
per-<code>char</code> processing can end up using a lot more memory:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let s = String::from(&quot;love: ❤️&quot;);
let v: Vec&lt;char&gt; = s.chars().collect();

assert_eq!(12, size_of_val(&amp;s[..]));
assert_eq!(32, size_of_val(&amp;v[..]));
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type.builtin">{`char`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`,`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
\ \ \ \ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs::S
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">Cons(char, Vec&lt;S&gt;)
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>size = 32 (0x20), align = 0x8, needs Drop</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span id="def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_13_4" className="constructor">{`Cons`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">char
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>A character type.</p>
<p>The <code>char</code> type represents a single character. More specifically, since<br>
'character' isn't a well-defined concept in Unicode, <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode<br>
scalar value</a>'.</p>
<p>This documentation describes a number of methods and trait implementations on the<br>
<code>char</code> type. For technical reasons, there is additional, separate<br>
documentation in <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/core/char/index.html">the <code>std::char</code> module</a> as well.</p>
<h1 id="validity-and-layout">Validity and Layout</h1>
<p>A <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode scalar value</a>', which is any '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#code_point">Unicode code point</a>'<br>
other than a <a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#surrogate_code_point">surrogate code point</a>. This has a fixed numerical definition:<br>
code points are in the range 0 to 0x10FFFF, inclusive.<br>
Surrogate code points, used by UTF-16, are in the range 0xD800 to 0xDFFF.</p>
<p>No <code>char</code> may be constructed, whether as a literal or at runtime, that is not a<br>
Unicode scalar value. Violating this rule causes undefined behavior.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Each of these is a compiler error

</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Panics; from_u32 returns None.

</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Undefined behavior

</code></pre>
<p>Unicode scalar values are also the exact set of values that may be encoded in UTF-8. Because<br>
<code>char</code> values are Unicode scalar values and functions may assume <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/core/primitive.str.html#invariant">incoming <code>str</code> values are<br>
valid UTF-8</a>, it is safe to store any <code>char</code> in a <code>str</code> or read<br>
any character from a <code>str</code> as a <code>char</code>.</p>
<p>The gap in valid <code>char</code> values is understood by the compiler, so in the<br>
below example the two ranges are understood to cover the whole range of<br>
possible <code>char</code> values and there is no error for a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/book/ch06-02-match.html#matches-are-exhaustive">non-exhaustive match</a>.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let c: char = 'a';

    '\\0' ..= '\\u{D7FF}' =&gt; false,
    '\\u{E000}' ..= '\\u{10FFFF}' =&gt; true,
};
</code></pre>
<p>All Unicode scalar values are valid <code>char</code> values, but not all of them represent a real<br>
character. Many Unicode scalar values are not currently assigned to a character, but may be in<br>
the future (&quot;reserved&quot;); some will never be a character (&quot;noncharacters&quot;); and some may be given<br>
different meanings by different users (&quot;private use&quot;).</p>
<p><code>char</code> is guaranteed to have the same size, alignment, and function call ABI as <code>u32</code> on all<br>
platforms.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::alloc::Layout;
assert_eq!(Layout::new::&lt;char&gt;(), Layout::new::&lt;u32&gt;());
</code></pre>
<h1 id="representation">Representation</h1>
<p><code>char</code> is always four bytes in size. This is a different representation than<br>
a given character would have as part of a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/std/string/struct.String.html"><code>String</code></a>. For example:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec!['h', 'e', 'l', 'l', 'o'];

// five elements times four bytes for each element
assert_eq!(20, v.len() * size_of::&lt;char&gt;());

let s = String::from(&quot;hello&quot;);

// five elements times one byte per element
assert_eq!(5, s.len() * size_of::&lt;u8&gt;());
</code></pre>
<p>As always, remember that a human intuition for 'character' might not map to<br>
Unicode's definitions. For example, despite looking similar, the 'é'<br>
character is one Unicode code point while 'é' is two Unicode code points:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut chars = &quot;é&quot;.chars();
// U+00e9: 'latin small letter e with acute'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{00e9}'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(None, chars.next());

let mut chars = &quot;é&quot;.chars();
// U+0065: 'latin small letter e'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0065}'), chars.next());
// U+0301: 'combining acute accent'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0301}'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(None, chars.next());
</code></pre>
<p>This means that the contents of the first string above <em>will</em> fit into a<br>
<code>char</code> while the contents of the second string <em>will not</em>. Trying to create<br>
a <code>char</code> literal with the contents of the second string gives an error:</p>
<pre><code class="language-text">error: character literal may only contain one codepoint: 'é'
let c = 'é';
        ^^^
</code></pre>
<p>Another implication of the 4-byte fixed size of a <code>char</code> is that<br>
per-<code>char</code> processing can end up using a lot more memory:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let s = String::from(&quot;love: ❤️&quot;);
let v: Vec&lt;char&gt; = s.chars().collect();

assert_eq!(12, size_of_val(&amp;s[..]));
assert_eq!(32, size_of_val(&amp;v[..]));
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type.builtin">{`char`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`,`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">alloc::vec
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub struct Vec&lt;T, A = Global&gt;
where
    A: Allocator,
{
    buf: RawVec&lt;T, A&gt;,
    len: usize,
}
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>A contiguous growable array type, written as <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code>, short for 'vector'.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut vec = Vec::new();
vec.push(1);
vec.push(2);

assert_eq!(vec.len(), 2);
assert_eq!(vec[0], 1);

assert_eq!(vec.pop(), Some(2));
assert_eq!(vec.len(), 1);

vec[0] = 7;
assert_eq!(vec[0], 7);

vec.extend([1, 2, 3]);

for x in &amp;vec {
    println!(&quot;{x}&quot;);
}
assert_eq!(vec, [7, 1, 2, 3]);
</code></pre>
<p>The <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/macros/macro.vec.html"><code>vec</code></a> macro is provided for convenient initialization:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut vec1 = vec![1, 2, 3];
vec1.push(4);
let vec2 = Vec::from([1, 2, 3, 4]);
assert_eq!(vec1, vec2);
</code></pre>
<p>It can also initialize each element of a <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code> with a given value.<br>
This may be more efficient than performing allocation and initialization<br>
in separate steps, especially when initializing a vector of zeros:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let vec = vec![0; 5];
assert_eq!(vec, [0, 0, 0, 0, 0]);

// The following is equivalent, but potentially slower:
let mut vec = Vec::with_capacity(5);
vec.resize(5, 0);
assert_eq!(vec, [0, 0, 0, 0, 0]);
</code></pre>
<p>For more information, see<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#capacity-and-reallocation">Capacity and Reallocation</a>.</p>
<p>Use a <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code> as an efficient stack:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut stack = Vec::new();

stack.push(1);
stack.push(2);
stack.push(3);

while let Some(top) = stack.pop() {
    // Prints 3, 2, 1
    println!(&quot;{top}&quot;);
}
</code></pre>
<h1 id="indexing">Indexing</h1>
<p>The <code>Vec</code> type allows access to values by index, because it implements the<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/ops/index/trait.Index.html"><code>Index</code></a> trait. An example will be more explicit:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![0, 2, 4, 6];
println!(&quot;{}&quot;, v[1]); // it will display '2'
</code></pre>
<p>However be careful: if you try to access an index which isn't in the <code>Vec</code>,<br>
your software will panic! You cannot do this:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![0, 2, 4, 6];
println!(&quot;{}&quot;, v[6]); // it will panic!
</code></pre>
<p>Use [<code>get</code>] and [<code>get_mut</code>] if you want to check whether the index is in<br>
the <code>Vec</code>.</p>
<h1 id="slicing">Slicing</h1>
<p>A <code>Vec</code> can be mutable. On the other hand, slices are read-only objects.<br>
To get a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/slice/index.html">slice</a>, use <a href="%60&amp;%60"><code>&amp;</code></a>. Example:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">fn read_slice(slice: &amp;[usize]) {
    // ...
}

let v = vec![0, 1];
read_slice(&amp;v);

// ... and that's all!
// you can also do it like this:
let u: &amp;[usize] = &amp;v;
// or like this:
let u: &amp;[_] = &amp;v;
</code></pre>
<p>In Rust, it's more common to pass slices as arguments rather than vectors<br>
when you just want to provide read access. The same goes for [<code>String</code>] and<br>
[<code>&amp;str</code>].</p>
<h1 id="capacity-and-reallocation">Capacity and reallocation</h1>
<p>The capacity of a vector is the amount of space allocated for any future<br>
elements that will be added onto the vector. This is not to be confused with<br>
the <em>length</em> of a vector, which specifies the number of actual elements<br>
within the vector. If a vector's length exceeds its capacity, its capacity<br>
will automatically be increased, but its elements will have to be<br>
reallocated.</p>
<p>For example, a vector with capacity 10 and length 0 would be an empty vector<br>
with space for 10 more elements. Pushing 10 or fewer elements onto the<br>
vector will not change its capacity or cause reallocation to occur. However,<br>
if the vector's length is increased to 11, it will have to reallocate, which<br>
can be slow. For this reason, it is recommended to use <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.with_capacity"><code>Vec::with_capacity</code></a><br>
whenever possible to specify how big the vector is expected to get.</p>
<h1 id="guarantees">Guarantees</h1>
<p>Due to its incredibly fundamental nature, <code>Vec</code> makes a lot of guarantees<br>
about its design. This ensures that it's as low-overhead as possible in<br>
the general case, and can be correctly manipulated in primitive ways<br>
by unsafe code. Note that these guarantees refer to an unqualified <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code>.<br>
If additional type parameters are added (e.g., to support custom allocators),<br>
overriding their defaults may change the behavior.</p>
<p>Most fundamentally, <code>Vec</code> is and always will be a (pointer, capacity, length)<br>
triplet. No more, no less. The order of these fields is completely<br>
unspecified, and you should use the appropriate methods to modify these.<br>
The pointer will never be null, so this type is null-pointer-optimized.</p>
<p>However, the pointer might not actually point to allocated memory. In particular,<br>
if you construct a <code>Vec</code> with capacity 0 via <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.new"><code>Vec::new</code></a>, <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/macros/macro.vec.html"><code>vec![]</code></a>,<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.with_capacity"><code>Vec::with_capacity(0)</code></a>, or by calling [<code>shrink_to_fit</code>]<br>
on an empty Vec, it will not allocate memory. Similarly, if you store zero-sized<br>
types inside a <code>Vec</code>, it will not allocate space for them. <em>Note that in this case<br>
the <code>Vec</code> might not report a [<code>capacity</code>] of 0</em>. <code>Vec</code> will allocate if and only<br>
if <code><br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/mem/fn.size_of.html">size_of::&lt;T&gt;</a>() * <a href="">capacity</a> &gt; 0</code>. In general, <code>Vec</code>'s allocation<br>
details are very subtle --- if you intend to allocate memory using a <code>Vec</code><br>
and use it for something else (either to pass to unsafe code, or to build your<br>
own memory-backed collection), be sure to deallocate this memory by using<br>
<code>from_raw_parts</code> to recover the <code>Vec</code> and then dropping it.</p>
<p>If a <code>Vec</code> <em>has</em> allocated memory, then the memory it points to is on the heap<br>
(as defined by the allocator Rust is configured to use by default), and its<br>
pointer points to [<code>len</code>] initialized, contiguous elements in order (what<br>
you would see if you coerced it to a slice), followed by <code><br>
[capacity] - [len]</code><br>
logically uninitialized, contiguous elements.</p>
<p>A vector containing the elements <code>'a'</code> and <code>'b'</code> with capacity 4 can be<br>
visualized as below. The top part is the <code>Vec</code> struct, it contains a<br>
pointer to the head of the allocation in the heap, length and capacity.<br>
The bottom part is the allocation on the heap, a contiguous memory block.</p>
<pre><code class="language-text">            ptr      len  capacity
       +--------+--------+--------+
       | 0x0123 |      2 |      4 |
       +--------+--------+--------+
            |
            v
Heap   +--------+--------+--------+--------+
       |    'a' |    'b' | uninit | uninit |
       +--------+--------+--------+--------+
</code></pre>
<ul>
<li><strong>uninit</strong> represents memory that is not initialized, see [<code>MaybeUninit</code>].</li>
<li>Note: the ABI is not stable and <code>Vec</code> makes no guarantees about its memory<br>
layout (including the order of fields).</li>
</ul>
<p><code>Vec</code> will never perform a &quot;small optimization&quot; where elements are actually<br>
stored on the stack for two reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>It would make it more difficult for unsafe code to correctly manipulate<br>
a <code>Vec</code>. The contents of a <code>Vec</code> wouldn't have a stable address if it were<br>
only moved, and it would be more difficult to determine if a <code>Vec</code> had<br>
actually allocated memory.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It would penalize the general case, incurring an additional branch<br>
on every access.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><code>Vec</code> will never automatically shrink itself, even if completely empty. This<br>
ensures no unnecessary allocations or deallocations occur. Emptying a <code>Vec</code><br>
and then filling it back up to the same [<code>len</code>] should incur no calls to<br>
the allocator. If you wish to free up unused memory, use<br>
[<code>shrink_to_fit</code>] or [<code>shrink_to</code>].</p>
<p>[<code>push</code>] and [<code>insert</code>] will never (re)allocate if the reported capacity is<br>
sufficient. [<code>push</code>] and [<code>insert</code>] <em>will</em> (re)allocate if<br>
<code><br>
[len] == [capacity]</code>. That is, the reported capacity is completely<br>
accurate, and can be relied on. It can even be used to manually free the memory<br>
allocated by a <code>Vec</code> if desired. Bulk insertion methods <em>may</em> reallocate, even<br>
when not necessary.</p>
<p><code>Vec</code> does not guarantee any particular growth strategy when reallocating<br>
when full, nor when [<code>reserve</code>] is called. The current strategy is basic<br>
and it may prove desirable to use a non-constant growth factor. Whatever<br>
strategy is used will of course guarantee <em>O</em>(1) amortized [<code>push</code>].</p>
<p>It is guaranteed, in order to respect the intentions of the programmer, that<br>
all of <code>vec![e_1, e_2, ..., e_n]</code>, <code>vec![x; n]</code>, and [<code>Vec::with_capacity(n)</code>] produce a <code>Vec</code><br>
that requests an allocation of the exact size needed for precisely <code>n</code> elements from the allocator,<br>
and no other size (such as, for example: a size rounded up to the nearest power of 2).<br>
The allocator will return an allocation that is at least as large as requested, but it may be larger.</p>
<p>It is guaranteed that the [<code>Vec::capacity</code>] method returns a value that is at least the requested capacity<br>
and not more than the allocated capacity.</p>
<p>The method <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.shrink_to_fit"><code>Vec::shrink_to_fit</code></a> will attempt to discard excess capacity an allocator has given to a <code>Vec</code>.<br>
If <code><br>
[len] == [capacity]</code>, then a <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code> can be converted<br>
to and from a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/boxed/struct.Box.html"><code>Box&lt;[T]&gt;</code></a> without reallocating or moving the elements.<br>
<code>Vec</code> exploits this fact as much as reasonable when implementing common conversions<br>
such as [<code>into_boxed_slice</code>].</p>
<p><code>Vec</code> will not specifically overwrite any data that is removed from it,<br>
but also won't specifically preserve it. Its uninitialized memory is<br>
scratch space that it may use however it wants. It will generally just do<br>
whatever is most efficient or otherwise easy to implement. Do not rely on<br>
removed data to be erased for security purposes. Even if you drop a <code>Vec</code>, its<br>
buffer may simply be reused by another allocation. Even if you zero a <code>Vec</code>'s memory<br>
first, that might not actually happen because the optimizer does not consider<br>
this a side-effect that must be preserved. There is one case which we will<br>
not break, however: using <code>unsafe</code> code to write to the excess capacity,<br>
and then increasing the length to match, is always valid.</p>
<p>Currently, <code>Vec</code> does not guarantee the order in which elements are dropped.<br>
The order has changed in the past and may change again.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type">{`Vec`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`<`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">enum S {
    Atom( /* … */ ),
    Cons( /* … */ ),
}
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><a href="#def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_11_5" className="type">{`S`}</a></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`>`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`,`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`}`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`

`}</span><span className="keyword">{`impl`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ fmt`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`::`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::fmt
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub trait Display
where
    Self: PointeeSized,
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Format trait for an empty format, <code>{}</code>.</p>
<p>Implementing this trait for a type will automatically implement the<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/string/trait.ToString.html"><code>ToString</code></a> trait for the type, allowing the usage<br>
of the <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/string/trait.ToString.html#tymethod.to_string"><code>.to_string()</code></a> method. Prefer implementing<br>
the <code>Display</code> trait for a type, rather than <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/string/trait.ToString.html"><code>ToString</code></a>.</p>
<p><code>Display</code> is similar to <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/fmt/trait.Debug.html"><code>Debug</code></a>, but <code>Display</code> is for user-facing<br>
output, and so cannot be derived.</p>
<p>For more information on formatters, see <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/fmt/index.html">the module-level documentation</a>.</p>
<h1 id="completeness-and-parseability">Completeness and parseability</h1>
<p><code>Display</code> for a type might not necessarily be a lossless or complete representation of the type.<br>
It may omit internal state, precision, or other information the type does not consider important<br>
for user-facing output, as determined by the type. As such, the output of <code>Display</code> might not be<br>
possible to parse, and even if it is, the result of parsing might not exactly match the original<br>
value.</p>
<p>However, if a type has a lossless <code>Display</code> implementation whose output is meant to be<br>
conveniently machine-parseable and not just meant for human consumption, then the type may wish<br>
to accept the same format in <code>FromStr</code>, and document that usage. Having both <code>Display</code> and<br>
<code>FromStr</code> implementations where the result of <code>Display</code> cannot be parsed with <code>FromStr</code> may<br>
surprise users.</p>
<h1 id="internationalization">Internationalization</h1>
<p>Because a type can only have one <code>Display</code> implementation, it is often preferable<br>
to only implement <code>Display</code> when there is a single most &quot;obvious&quot; way that<br>
values can be formatted as text. This could mean formatting according to the<br>
&quot;invariant&quot; culture and &quot;undefined&quot; locale, or it could mean that the type<br>
display is designed for a specific culture/locale, such as developer logs.</p>
<p>If not all values have a justifiably canonical textual format or if you want<br>
to support alternative formats not covered by the standard set of possible<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/fmt/index.html#formatting-traits">formatting traits</a>, the most flexible approach is display adapters: methods<br>
like <a href="%60str::escape_default%60"><code>str::escape_default</code></a> or <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/path/struct.Path.html#method.display"><code>Path::display</code></a> which create a wrapper<br>
implementing <code>Display</code> to output the specific display format.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<p>Implementing <code>Display</code> on a type:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::fmt;

struct Point {
    x: i32,
    y: i32,
}

impl fmt::Display for Point {
    fn fmt(&amp;self, f: &amp;mut fmt::Formatter&lt;'_&gt;) -&gt; fmt::Result {
        write!(f, &quot;({}, {})&quot;, self.x, self.y)
    }
}

let origin = Point { x: 0, y: 0 };

assert_eq!(format!(&quot;The origin is: {origin}&quot;), &quot;The origin is: (0, 0)&quot;);
</code></pre>

}
</code></pre>

<hr>
<p>Configuration for formatting.</p>
<p>A <code>Formatter</code> represents various options related to formatting. Users do not<br>
construct <code>Formatter</code>s directly; a mutable reference to one is passed to<br>
the <code>fmt</code> method of all formatting traits, like <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/fmt/trait.Debug.html"><code>Debug</code></a> and <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/fmt/trait.Display.html"><code>Display</code></a>.</p>
<p>To interact with a <code>Formatter</code>, you'll call various methods to change the<br>
various options related to formatting. For examples, please see the<br>
documentation of the methods defined on <code>Formatter</code> below.</p>

</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">macro_rules! write // matched arm #0
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Writes formatted data into a buffer.</p>
<p>This macro accepts a 'writer', a format string, and a list of arguments. Arguments will be<br>
formatted according to the specified format string and the result will be passed to the writer.<br>
The writer may be any value with a <code>write_fmt</code> method; generally this comes from an<br>
implementation of either the [<code>fmt::Write</code>] or the <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/std/io/trait.Write.html"><code>io::Write</code></a> trait. The macro<br>
returns whatever the <code>write_fmt</code> method returns; commonly a [<code>fmt::Result</code>], or an<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/std/io/type.Result.html"><code>io::Result</code></a>.</p>
<p>See <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/std/fmt/index.html"><code>std::fmt</code></a> for more information on the format string syntax.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::io::Write;

fn main() -&gt; std::io::Result&lt;()&gt; {
    let mut w = Vec::new();
    write!(&amp;mut w, &quot;test&quot;)?;
    write!(&amp;mut w, &quot;formatted {}&quot;, &quot;arguments&quot;)?;

    assert_eq!(w, b&quot;testformatted arguments&quot;);
    Ok(())
}
</code></pre>
<p>A module can import both <code>std::fmt::Write</code> and <code>std::io::Write</code> and call <code>write!</code> on objects<br>
implementing either, as objects do not typically implement both. However, the module must<br>
avoid conflict between the trait names, such as by importing them as <code>_</code> or otherwise renaming<br>
them:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::fmt::Write as _;
use std::io::Write as _;

fn main() -&gt; Result&lt;(), Box&lt;dyn std::error::Error&gt;&gt; {
    let mut s = String::new();
    let mut v = Vec::new();

    write!(&amp;mut s, &quot;{} {}&quot;, &quot;abc&quot;, 123)?; // uses fmt::Write::write_fmt
    write!(&amp;mut v, &quot;s = {:?}&quot;, s)?; // uses io::Write::write_fmt
    assert_eq!(v, b&quot;s = \\&quot;abc 123\\&quot;&quot;);
    Ok(())
}
</code></pre>
<p>If you also need the trait names themselves, such as to implement one or both on your types,<br>
import the containing module and then name them with a prefix:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::fmt::{self, Write as _};
use std::io::{self, Write as _};

struct Example;

impl fmt::Write for Example {
    fn write_str(&amp;mut self, _s: &amp;str) -&gt; core::fmt::Result {
         unimplemented!();
    }
}
</code></pre>
<p>Note: This macro can be used in <code>no_std</code> setups as well.<br>
In a <code>no_std</code> setup you are responsible for the implementation details of the components.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use core::fmt::Write;

struct Example;

impl Write for Example {
    fn write_str(&amp;mut self, _s: &amp;str) -&gt; core::fmt::Result {
         unimplemented!();
    }
}

let mut m = Example{};
write!(&amp;mut m, &quot;Hello World&quot;).expect(&quot;Not written&quot;);
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.macro">{`write`}</span></Tooltip><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::macros

<hr>
<p>Writes formatted data into a buffer.</p>
<p>This macro accepts a 'writer', a format string, and a list of arguments. Arguments will be<br>
formatted according to the specified format string and the result will be passed to the writer.<br>
The writer may be any value with a <code>write_fmt</code> method; generally this comes from an<br>

<pre><code class="language-rust">enum S {
    Atom( /* … */ ),
    Cons( /* … */ ),
}
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><a href="#def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_11_5" className="type">{`S`}</a></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`::`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs::S
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">Cons(char, Vec&lt;S&gt;)
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><a href="#def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_13_4" className="constructor">{`Cons`}</a></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`head`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`,`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ rest`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ =>\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`{`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::macros
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">macro_rules! write // matched arm #0
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Writes formatted data into a buffer.</p>
<p>This macro accepts a 'writer', a format string, and a list of arguments. Arguments will be<br>
formatted according to the specified format string and the result will be passed to the writer.<br>
The writer may be any value with a <code>write_fmt</code> method; generally this comes from an<br>
implementation of either the [<code>fmt::Write</code>] or the <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/std/io/trait.Write.html"><code>io::Write</code></a> trait. The macro<br>
returns whatever the <code>write_fmt</code> method returns; commonly a [<code>fmt::Result</code>], or an<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/std/io/type.Result.html"><code>io::Result</code></a>.</p>
<p>See <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/std/fmt/index.html"><code>std::fmt</code></a> for more information on the format string syntax.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::io::Write;

fn main() -&gt; std::io::Result&lt;()&gt; {
    let mut w = Vec::new();
    write!(&amp;mut w, &quot;test&quot;)?;
    write!(&amp;mut w, &quot;formatted {}&quot;, &quot;arguments&quot;)?;

    assert_eq!(w, b&quot;testformatted arguments&quot;);
    Ok(())
}
</code></pre>
<p>A module can import both <code>std::fmt::Write</code> and <code>std::io::Write</code> and call <code>write!</code> on objects<br>
implementing either, as objects do not typically implement both. However, the module must<br>
avoid conflict between the trait names, such as by importing them as <code>_</code> or otherwise renaming<br>
them:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::fmt::Write as _;
use std::io::Write as _;

fn main() -&gt; Result&lt;(), Box&lt;dyn std::error::Error&gt;&gt; {
    let mut s = String::new();
    let mut v = Vec::new();

    write!(&amp;mut s, &quot;{} {}&quot;, &quot;abc&quot;, 123)?; // uses fmt::Write::write_fmt
    write!(&amp;mut v, &quot;s = {:?}&quot;, s)?; // uses io::Write::write_fmt
    assert_eq!(v, b&quot;s = \\&quot;abc 123\\&quot;&quot;);
    Ok(())
}
</code></pre>
<p>If you also need the trait names themselves, such as to implement one or both on your types,<br>
import the containing module and then name them with a prefix:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::fmt::{self, Write as _};
use std::io::{self, Write as _};

struct Example;

impl fmt::Write for Example {
    fn write_str(&amp;mut self, _s: &amp;str) -&gt; core::fmt::Result {
         unimplemented!();
    }
}
</code></pre>
<p>Note: This macro can be used in <code>no_std</code> setups as well.<br>
In a <code>no_std</code> setup you are responsible for the implementation details of the components.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use core::fmt::Write;

struct Example;

impl Write for Example {
    fn write_str(&amp;mut self, _s: &amp;str) -&gt; core::fmt::Result {
         unimplemented!();
    }
}

let mut m = Example{};
write!(&amp;mut m, &quot;Hello World&quot;).expect(&quot;Not written&quot;);
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.macro">{`write`}</span></Tooltip><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::macros

</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>A character type.</p>
<p>The <code>char</code> type represents a single character. More specifically, since<br>
'character' isn't a well-defined concept in Unicode, <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode<br>
scalar value</a>'.</p>
<p>This documentation describes a number of methods and trait implementations on the<br>
<code>char</code> type. For technical reasons, there is additional, separate<br>
documentation in <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/core/char/index.html">the <code>std::char</code> module</a> as well.</p>
<h1 id="validity-and-layout">Validity and Layout</h1>
<p>A <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode scalar value</a>', which is any '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#code_point">Unicode code point</a>'<br>
other than a <a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#surrogate_code_point">surrogate code point</a>. This has a fixed numerical definition:<br>
code points are in the range 0 to 0x10FFFF, inclusive.<br>
Surrogate code points, used by UTF-16, are in the range 0xD800 to 0xDFFF.</p>
<p>No <code>char</code> may be constructed, whether as a literal or at runtime, that is not a<br>
Unicode scalar value. Violating this rule causes undefined behavior.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Each of these is a compiler error
['\\u{D800}', '\\u{DFFF}', '\\u{110000}'];
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Panics; from_u32 returns None.
char::from_u32(0xDE01).unwrap();
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Undefined behavior
let _ = unsafe { char::from_u32_unchecked(0x110000) };
</code></pre>
<p>Unicode scalar values are also the exact set of values that may be encoded in UTF-8. Because<br>
<code>char</code> values are Unicode scalar values and functions may assume <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/core/primitive.str.html#invariant">incoming <code>str</code> values are<br>
valid UTF-8</a>, it is safe to store any <code>char</code> in a <code>str</code> or read<br>
any character from a <code>str</code> as a <code>char</code>.</p>
<p>The gap in valid <code>char</code> values is understood by the compiler, so in the<br>
below example the two ranges are understood to cover the whole range of<br>
possible <code>char</code> values and there is no error for a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/book/ch06-02-match.html#matches-are-exhaustive">non-exhaustive match</a>.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let c: char = 'a';
match c {
    '\\0' ..= '\\u{D7FF}' =&gt; false,
    '\\u{E000}' ..= '\\u{10FFFF}' =&gt; true,
};
</code></pre>
<p>All Unicode scalar values are valid <code>char</code> values, but not all of them represent a real<br>
character. Many Unicode scalar values are not currently assigned to a character, but may be in<br>
the future (&quot;reserved&quot;); some will never be a character (&quot;noncharacters&quot;); and some may be given<br>
different meanings by different users (&quot;private use&quot;).</p>
<p><code>char</code> is guaranteed to have the same size, alignment, and function call ABI as <code>u32</code> on all<br>
platforms.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::alloc::Layout;
assert_eq!(Layout::new::&lt;char&gt;(), Layout::new::&lt;u32&gt;());
</code></pre>
<h1 id="representation">Representation</h1>
<p><code>char</code> is always four bytes in size. This is a different representation than<br>
a given character would have as part of a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/std/string/struct.String.html"><code>String</code></a>. For example:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec!['h', 'e', 'l', 'l', 'o'];

// five elements times four bytes for each element
assert_eq!(20, v.len() * size_of::&lt;char&gt;());

let s = String::from(&quot;hello&quot;);

// five elements times one byte per element
assert_eq!(5, s.len() * size_of::&lt;u8&gt;());
</code></pre>
<p>As always, remember that a human intuition for 'character' might not map to<br>
Unicode's definitions. For example, despite looking similar, the 'é'<br>
character is one Unicode code point while 'é' is two Unicode code points:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut chars = &quot;é&quot;.chars();
// U+00e9: 'latin small letter e with acute'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{00e9}'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(None, chars.next());

let mut chars = &quot;é&quot;.chars();
// U+0065: 'latin small letter e'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0065}'), chars.next());
// U+0301: 'combining acute accent'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0301}'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(None, chars.next());
</code></pre>
<p>This means that the contents of the first string above <em>will</em> fit into a<br>
<code>char</code> while the contents of the second string <em>will not</em>. Trying to create<br>
a <code>char</code> literal with the contents of the second string gives an error:</p>
<pre><code class="language-text">error: character literal may only contain one codepoint: 'é'
let c = 'é';
        ^^^
</code></pre>
<p>Another implication of the 4-byte fixed size of a <code>char</code> is that<br>
per-<code>char</code> processing can end up using a lot more memory:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let s = String::from(&quot;love: ❤️&quot;);
let v: Vec&lt;char&gt; = s.chars().collect();

assert_eq!(12, size_of_val(&amp;s[..]));
assert_eq!(32, size_of_val(&amp;v[..]));
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type.builtin">{`char`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`,`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
\ \ \ \ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs::Token
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">Op(char)
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>size = 8, align = 0x4, no Drop</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span id="def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_34_4" className="constructor">{`Op`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">char
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>A character type.</p>
<p>The <code>char</code> type represents a single character. More specifically, since<br>
'character' isn't a well-defined concept in Unicode, <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode<br>

</code></pre>
<h1 id="representation">Representation</h1>

let s = String::from(&quot;hello&quot;);

// five elements times one byte per element
assert_eq!(5, s.len() * size_of::&lt;u8&gt;());
</code></pre>

`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`}`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`

`}</span><span className="keyword">{`struct`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">struct Lexer {
    tokens: Vec&lt;Token&gt;,
}
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>size = 24 (0x18), align = 0x8, needs Drop</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span id="def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_38_7" className="type">{`Lexer`}</span></Tooltip><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`{`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
\ \ \ \ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs::Lexer
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">tokens: Vec&lt;Token, Global&gt;
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>size = 24 (0x18), align = 0x8, offset = 0x0, needs Drop</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span id="def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_39_4" className="property">{`tokens`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`:`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">alloc::vec
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub struct Vec&lt;T, A = Global&gt;
where
    A: Allocator,
{
    buf: RawVec&lt;T, A&gt;,
    len: usize,
}
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>A contiguous growable array type, written as <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code>, short for 'vector'.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut vec = Vec::new();
vec.push(1);
vec.push(2);

assert_eq!(vec.len(), 2);
assert_eq!(vec[0], 1);

assert_eq!(vec.pop(), Some(2));
assert_eq!(vec.len(), 1);

vec[0] = 7;
assert_eq!(vec[0], 7);

vec.extend([1, 2, 3]);

for x in &amp;vec {
    println!(&quot;{x}&quot;);
}
assert_eq!(vec, [7, 1, 2, 3]);
</code></pre>
<p>The <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/macros/macro.vec.html"><code>vec</code></a> macro is provided for convenient initialization:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut vec1 = vec![1, 2, 3];
vec1.push(4);
let vec2 = Vec::from([1, 2, 3, 4]);
assert_eq!(vec1, vec2);
</code></pre>
<p>It can also initialize each element of a <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code> with a given value.<br>
This may be more efficient than performing allocation and initialization<br>
in separate steps, especially when initializing a vector of zeros:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let vec = vec![0; 5];
assert_eq!(vec, [0, 0, 0, 0, 0]);

// The following is equivalent, but potentially slower:
let mut vec = Vec::with_capacity(5);
vec.resize(5, 0);
assert_eq!(vec, [0, 0, 0, 0, 0]);
</code></pre>
<p>For more information, see<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#capacity-and-reallocation">Capacity and Reallocation</a>.</p>
<p>Use a <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code> as an efficient stack:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut stack = Vec::new();

stack.push(1);
stack.push(2);
stack.push(3);

while let Some(top) = stack.pop() {
    // Prints 3, 2, 1
    println!(&quot;{top}&quot;);
}
</code></pre>
<h1 id="indexing">Indexing</h1>
<p>The <code>Vec</code> type allows access to values by index, because it implements the<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/ops/index/trait.Index.html"><code>Index</code></a> trait. An example will be more explicit:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![0, 2, 4, 6];
println!(&quot;{}&quot;, v[1]); // it will display '2'
</code></pre>
<p>However be careful: if you try to access an index which isn't in the <code>Vec</code>,<br>
your software will panic! You cannot do this:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![0, 2, 4, 6];
println!(&quot;{}&quot;, v[6]); // it will panic!
</code></pre>
<p>Use [<code>get</code>] and [<code>get_mut</code>] if you want to check whether the index is in<br>
the <code>Vec</code>.</p>
<h1 id="slicing">Slicing</h1>
<p>A <code>Vec</code> can be mutable. On the other hand, slices are read-only objects.<br>
To get a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/slice/index.html">slice</a>, use <a href="%60&amp;%60"><code>&amp;</code></a>. Example:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">fn read_slice(slice: &amp;[usize]) {
    // ...
}

let v = vec![0, 1];
read_slice(&amp;v);

// ... and that's all!
// you can also do it like this:
let u: &amp;[usize] = &amp;v;
// or like this:
let u: &amp;[_] = &amp;v;
</code></pre>
<p>In Rust, it's more common to pass slices as arguments rather than vectors<br>
when you just want to provide read access. The same goes for [<code>String</code>] and<br>
[<code>&amp;str</code>].</p>
<h1 id="capacity-and-reallocation">Capacity and reallocation</h1>
<p>The capacity of a vector is the amount of space allocated for any future<br>
elements that will be added onto the vector. This is not to be confused with<br>
the <em>length</em> of a vector, which specifies the number of actual elements<br>
within the vector. If a vector's length exceeds its capacity, its capacity<br>
will automatically be increased, but its elements will have to be<br>
reallocated.</p>
<p>For example, a vector with capacity 10 and length 0 would be an empty vector<br>
with space for 10 more elements. Pushing 10 or fewer elements onto the<br>
vector will not change its capacity or cause reallocation to occur. However,<br>
if the vector's length is increased to 11, it will have to reallocate, which<br>
can be slow. For this reason, it is recommended to use <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.with_capacity"><code>Vec::with_capacity</code></a><br>
whenever possible to specify how big the vector is expected to get.</p>
<h1 id="guarantees">Guarantees</h1>
<p>Due to its incredibly fundamental nature, <code>Vec</code> makes a lot of guarantees<br>
about its design. This ensures that it's as low-overhead as possible in<br>
the general case, and can be correctly manipulated in primitive ways<br>
by unsafe code. Note that these guarantees refer to an unqualified <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code>.<br>
If additional type parameters are added (e.g., to support custom allocators),<br>
overriding their defaults may change the behavior.</p>
<p>Most fundamentally, <code>Vec</code> is and always will be a (pointer, capacity, length)<br>
triplet. No more, no less. The order of these fields is completely<br>
unspecified, and you should use the appropriate methods to modify these.<br>
The pointer will never be null, so this type is null-pointer-optimized.</p>
<p>However, the pointer might not actually point to allocated memory. In particular,<br>
if you construct a <code>Vec</code> with capacity 0 via <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.new"><code>Vec::new</code></a>, <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/macros/macro.vec.html"><code>vec![]</code></a>,<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.with_capacity"><code>Vec::with_capacity(0)</code></a>, or by calling [<code>shrink_to_fit</code>]<br>
on an empty Vec, it will not allocate memory. Similarly, if you store zero-sized<br>
types inside a <code>Vec</code>, it will not allocate space for them. <em>Note that in this case<br>
the <code>Vec</code> might not report a [<code>capacity</code>] of 0</em>. <code>Vec</code> will allocate if and only<br>
if <code><br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/mem/fn.size_of.html">size_of::&lt;T&gt;</a>() * <a href="">capacity</a> &gt; 0</code>. In general, <code>Vec</code>'s allocation<br>
details are very subtle --- if you intend to allocate memory using a <code>Vec</code><br>
and use it for something else (either to pass to unsafe code, or to build your<br>
own memory-backed collection), be sure to deallocate this memory by using<br>
<code>from_raw_parts</code> to recover the <code>Vec</code> and then dropping it.</p>
<p>If a <code>Vec</code> <em>has</em> allocated memory, then the memory it points to is on the heap<br>
(as defined by the allocator Rust is configured to use by default), and its<br>
pointer points to [<code>len</code>] initialized, contiguous elements in order (what<br>
you would see if you coerced it to a slice), followed by <code><br>
[capacity] - [len]</code><br>
logically uninitialized, contiguous elements.</p>
<p>A vector containing the elements <code>'a'</code> and <code>'b'</code> with capacity 4 can be<br>
visualized as below. The top part is the <code>Vec</code> struct, it contains a<br>
pointer to the head of the allocation in the heap, length and capacity.<br>
The bottom part is the allocation on the heap, a contiguous memory block.</p>
<pre><code class="language-text">            ptr      len  capacity
       +--------+--------+--------+
       | 0x0123 |      2 |      4 |
       +--------+--------+--------+
            |
            v
Heap   +--------+--------+--------+--------+
       |    'a' |    'b' | uninit | uninit |
       +--------+--------+--------+--------+
</code></pre>
<ul>
<li><strong>uninit</strong> represents memory that is not initialized, see [<code>MaybeUninit</code>].</li>
<li>Note: the ABI is not stable and <code>Vec</code> makes no guarantees about its memory<br>
layout (including the order of fields).</li>
</ul>
<p><code>Vec</code> will never perform a &quot;small optimization&quot; where elements are actually<br>
stored on the stack for two reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>It would make it more difficult for unsafe code to correctly manipulate<br>
a <code>Vec</code>. The contents of a <code>Vec</code> wouldn't have a stable address if it were<br>
only moved, and it would be more difficult to determine if a <code>Vec</code> had<br>
actually allocated memory.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It would penalize the general case, incurring an additional branch<br>
on every access.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><code>Vec</code> will never automatically shrink itself, even if completely empty. This<br>
ensures no unnecessary allocations or deallocations occur. Emptying a <code>Vec</code><br>
and then filling it back up to the same [<code>len</code>] should incur no calls to<br>
the allocator. If you wish to free up unused memory, use<br>
[<code>shrink_to_fit</code>] or [<code>shrink_to</code>].</p>
<p>[<code>push</code>] and [<code>insert</code>] will never (re)allocate if the reported capacity is<br>
sufficient. [<code>push</code>] and [<code>insert</code>] <em>will</em> (re)allocate if<br>
<code><br>
[len] == [capacity]</code>. That is, the reported capacity is completely<br>
accurate, and can be relied on. It can even be used to manually free the memory<br>
allocated by a <code>Vec</code> if desired. Bulk insertion methods <em>may</em> reallocate, even<br>
when not necessary.</p>
<p><code>Vec</code> does not guarantee any particular growth strategy when reallocating<br>
when full, nor when [<code>reserve</code>] is called. The current strategy is basic<br>
and it may prove desirable to use a non-constant growth factor. Whatever<br>
strategy is used will of course guarantee <em>O</em>(1) amortized [<code>push</code>].</p>
<p>It is guaranteed, in order to respect the intentions of the programmer, that<br>
all of <code>vec![e_1, e_2, ..., e_n]</code>, <code>vec![x; n]</code>, and [<code>Vec::with_capacity(n)</code>] produce a <code>Vec</code><br>
that requests an allocation of the exact size needed for precisely <code>n</code> elements from the allocator,<br>
and no other size (such as, for example: a size rounded up to the nearest power of 2).<br>
The allocator will return an allocation that is at least as large as requested, but it may be larger.</p>
<p>It is guaranteed that the [<code>Vec::capacity</code>] method returns a value that is at least the requested capacity<br>
and not more than the allocated capacity.</p>
<p>The method <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.shrink_to_fit"><code>Vec::shrink_to_fit</code></a> will attempt to discard excess capacity an allocator has given to a <code>Vec</code>.<br>
If <code><br>
[len] == [capacity]</code>, then a <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code> can be converted<br>
to and from a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/boxed/struct.Box.html"><code>Box&lt;[T]&gt;</code></a> without reallocating or moving the elements.<br>
<code>Vec</code> exploits this fact as much as reasonable when implementing common conversions<br>
such as [<code>into_boxed_slice</code>].</p>
<p><code>Vec</code> will not specifically overwrite any data that is removed from it,<br>
but also won't specifically preserve it. Its uninitialized memory is<br>
scratch space that it may use however it wants. It will generally just do<br>
whatever is most efficient or otherwise easy to implement. Do not rely on<br>
removed data to be erased for security purposes. Even if you drop a <code>Vec</code>, its<br>
buffer may simply be reused by another allocation. Even if you zero a <code>Vec</code>'s memory<br>
first, that might not actually happen because the optimizer does not consider<br>
this a side-effect that must be preserved. There is one case which we will<br>
not break, however: using <code>unsafe</code> code to write to the excess capacity,<br>
and then increasing the length to match, is always valid.</p>
<p>Currently, <code>Vec</code> does not guarantee the order in which elements are dropped.<br>
The order has changed in the past and may change again.</p>

</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>String slices.</p>
<p><em><a href="crate::str">See also the <code>std::str</code> module</a>.</em></p>
<p>The <code>str</code> type, also called a 'string slice', is the most primitive string<br>
type. It is usually seen in its borrowed form, <code>&amp;str</code>. It is also the type<br>
of string literals, <code>&amp;'static str</code>.</p>
<h1 id="basic-usage">Basic Usage</h1>
<p>String literals are string slices:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let hello_world = &quot;Hello, World!&quot;;
</code></pre>
<p>Here we have declared a string slice initialized with a string literal.<br>
String literals have a static lifetime, which means the string <code>hello_world</code><br>
is guaranteed to be valid for the duration of the entire program.<br>
We can explicitly specify <code>hello_world</code>'s lifetime as well:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let hello_world: &amp;'static str = &quot;Hello, world!&quot;;
</code></pre>
<h1 id="representation">Representation</h1>
<p>A <code>&amp;str</code> is made up of two components: a pointer to some bytes, and a<br>
length. You can look at these with the [<code>as_ptr</code>] and [<code>len</code>] methods:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::slice;
use std::str;

let story = &quot;Once upon a time...&quot;;

let ptr = story.as_ptr();
let len = story.len();

// story has nineteen bytes
assert_eq!(19, len);

// We can re-build a str out of ptr and len. This is all unsafe because
// we are responsible for making sure the two components are valid:
let s = unsafe {
    // First, we build a &amp;[u8]...
    let slice = slice::from_raw_parts(ptr, len);

    // ... and then convert that slice into a string slice
    str::from_utf8(slice)
};

assert_eq!(s, Ok(story));
</code></pre>
<p>Note: This example shows the internals of <code>&amp;str</code>. <code>unsafe</code> should not be<br>
used to get a string slice under normal circumstances. Use <code>as_str</code><br>
instead.</p>
<h1 id="invariant">Invariant</h1>
<p>Rust libraries may assume that string slices are always valid UTF-8.</p>
<p>Constructing a non-UTF-8 string slice is not immediate undefined behavior, but any function<br>
called on a string slice may assume that it is valid UTF-8, which means that a non-UTF-8 string<br>
slice can lead to undefined behavior down the road.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type.builtin">{`str`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ ->\ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs
</code></pre>

    tokens: Vec&lt;Token&gt;,
}
</code></pre>

</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub fn chars(&amp;self) -&gt; Chars&lt;'_&gt;
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Returns an iterator over the [<code>char</code>]s of a string slice.</p>
<p>As a string slice consists of valid UTF-8, we can iterate through a<br>
string slice by [<code>char</code>]. This method returns such an iterator.</p>
<p>It's important to remember that [<code>char</code>] represents a Unicode Scalar<br>
Value, and might not match your idea of what a 'character' is. Iteration<br>
over grapheme clusters may be what you actually want. This functionality<br>
is not provided by Rust's standard library, check crates.io instead.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<p>Basic usage:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let word = &quot;goodbye&quot;;

let count = word.chars().count();
assert_eq!(7, count);

let mut chars = word.chars();

assert_eq!(Some('g'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(Some('o'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(Some('o'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(Some('d'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(Some('b'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(Some('y'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(Some('e'), chars.next());

assert_eq!(None, chars.next());
</code></pre>
<p>Remember, [<code>char</code>]s might not match your intuition about characters:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let y = &quot;y̆&quot;;

let mut chars = y.chars();

assert_eq!(Some('y'), chars.next()); // not 'y̆'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0306}'), chars.next());

assert_eq!(None, chars.next());
</code></pre>

</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub trait Iterator
pub fn filter&lt;P&gt;(self, predicate: P) -&gt; Filter&lt;Self, P&gt;
where
    Self: Sized,
    P: FnMut(&amp;Self::Item) -&gt; bool,
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p><code>Self</code> = <code>Chars&lt;'_&gt;</code>, <code>P</code> = <code>impl FnMut(&amp;char) -&gt; …</code></p>
<hr>
<p>Creates an iterator which uses a closure to determine if an element<br>
should be yielded.</p>
<p>Given an element the closure must return <code>true</code> or <code>false</code>. The returned<br>
iterator will yield only the elements for which the closure returns<br>
<code>true</code>.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<p>Basic usage:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let a = [0i32, 1, 2];

let mut iter = a.into_iter().filter(|x| x.is_positive());

assert_eq!(iter.next(), Some(1));
assert_eq!(iter.next(), Some(2));
assert_eq!(iter.next(), None);
</code></pre>
<p>Because the closure passed to <code>filter()</code> takes a reference, and many<br>
iterators iterate over references, this leads to a possibly confusing<br>
situation, where the type of the closure is a double reference:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let s = &amp;[0, 1, 2];

let mut iter = s.iter().filter(|x| **x &gt; 1); // needs two *s!

assert_eq!(iter.next(), Some(&amp;2));
assert_eq!(iter.next(), None);
</code></pre>
<p>It's common to instead use destructuring on the argument to strip away one:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let s = &amp;[0, 1, 2];

let mut iter = s.iter().filter(|&amp;x| *x &gt; 1); // both &amp; and *

assert_eq!(iter.next(), Some(&amp;2));
assert_eq!(iter.next(), None);
</code></pre>
<p>or both:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let s = &amp;[0, 1, 2];

let mut iter = s.iter().filter(|&amp;&amp;x| x &gt; 1); // two &amp;s

assert_eq!(iter.next(), Some(&amp;2));
assert_eq!(iter.next(), None);
</code></pre>
<p>of these layers.</p>
<p>Note that <code>iter.filter(f).next()</code> is equivalent to <code>iter.find(f)</code>.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.method">{`filter`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`|it|\ !it`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`.`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::char::methods
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub const fn is_ascii_whitespace(&amp;self) -&gt; bool
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Checks if the value is an ASCII whitespace character:<br>
U+0020 SPACE, U+0009 HORIZONTAL TAB, U+000A LINE FEED,<br>
U+000C FORM FEED, or U+000D CARRIAGE RETURN.</p>
<p>Rust uses the WhatWG Infra Standard's <a href="https://infra.spec.whatwg.org/#ascii-whitespace">definition of ASCII<br>
whitespace</a>. There are several other definitions in<br>
wide use. For instance, <a href="https://pubs.opengroup.org/onlinepubs/9699919799/basedefs/V1_chap07.html#tag_07_03_01">the POSIX locale</a> includes<br>
U+000B VERTICAL TAB as well as all the above characters,<br>
but—from the very same specification—<a href="https://pubs.opengroup.org/onlinepubs/9699919799/utilities/V3_chap02.html#tag_18_06_05">the default rule for<br>
&quot;field splitting&quot; in the Bourne shell</a> considers <em>only</em><br>
SPACE, HORIZONTAL TAB, and LINE FEED as whitespace.</p>
<p>If you are writing a program that will process an existing<br>
file format, check what that format's definition of whitespace is<br>
before using this function.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let uppercase_a = 'A';
let uppercase_g = 'G';
let a = 'a';
let g = 'g';
let zero = '0';
let percent = '%';
let space = ' ';
let lf = '\\n';
let esc = '\\x1b';

assert!(!uppercase_a.is_ascii_whitespace());
assert!(!uppercase_g.is_ascii_whitespace());
assert!(!a.is_ascii_whitespace());
assert!(!g.is_ascii_whitespace());
assert!(!zero.is_ascii_whitespace());
assert!(!percent.is_ascii_whitespace());
assert!(space.is_ascii_whitespace());
assert!(lf.is_ascii_whitespace());
assert!(!esc.is_ascii_whitespace());
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.method">{`is_ascii_whitespace`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ `}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`.`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::iter::traits::iterator::Iterator
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub trait Iterator
pub fn map&lt;B, F&gt;(self, f: F) -&gt; Map&lt;Self, F&gt;
where
    Self: Sized,
    F: FnMut(Self::Item) -&gt; B,
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p><code>Self</code> = <code>Filter&lt;Chars&lt;'_&gt;, impl FnMut(…) -&gt; …&gt;</code>, <code>B</code> = <code>Token</code>, <code>F</code> = <code>impl FnMut(char) -&gt; …</code></p>
<hr>
<p>Takes a closure and creates an iterator which calls that closure on each<br>
element.</p>
<p><code>map()</code> transforms one iterator into another, by means of its argument:<br>
something that implements <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/ops/function/trait.FnMut.html"><code>FnMut</code></a>. It produces a new iterator which<br>
calls this closure on each element of the original iterator.</p>
<p>If you are good at thinking in types, you can think of <code>map()</code> like this:<br>
If you have an iterator that gives you elements of some type <code>A</code>, and<br>
you want an iterator of some other type <code>B</code>, you can use <code>map()</code>,<br>
passing a closure that takes an <code>A</code> and returns a <code>B</code>.</p>
<p><code>map()</code> is conceptually similar to a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/iter/book/ch03-05-control-flow.html#looping-through-a-collection-with-for"><code>for</code></a> loop. However, as <code>map()</code> is<br>
lazy, it is best used when you're already working with other iterators.<br>
If you're doing some sort of looping for a side effect, it's considered<br>
more idiomatic to use <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/iter/book/ch03-05-control-flow.html#looping-through-a-collection-with-for"><code>for</code></a> than <code>map()</code>.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<p>Basic usage:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let a = [1, 2, 3];

let mut iter = a.iter().map(|x| 2 * x);

assert_eq!(iter.next(), Some(2));
assert_eq!(iter.next(), Some(4));
assert_eq!(iter.next(), Some(6));
assert_eq!(iter.next(), None);
</code></pre>
<p>If you're doing some sort of side effect, prefer <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/iter/book/ch03-05-control-flow.html#looping-through-a-collection-with-for"><code>for</code></a> to <code>map()</code>:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// don't do this:
(0..5).map(|x| println!(&quot;{x}&quot;));

// it won't even execute, as it is lazy. Rust will warn you about this.

// Instead, use a for-loop:
for x in 0..5 {
    println!(&quot;{x}&quot;);
}
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.method">{`map`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`|c|\ `}</span><span className="keyword">{`match`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ c\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`{`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
