\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ `}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`.`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::iter::traits::iterator::Iterator
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub trait Iterator
pub fn collect&lt;B&gt;(self) -&gt; B
where
    B: FromIterator&lt;Self::Item&gt;,
    Self: Sized,
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p><code>Self</code> = <code>Map&lt;Filter&lt;Chars&lt;'_&gt;, …&gt;, …&gt;</code>, <code>B</code> = <code>Vec&lt;Token&gt;</code></p>
<hr>
<p>Transforms an iterator into a collection.</p>
<p><code>collect()</code> can take anything iterable, and turn it into a relevant<br>
collection. This is one of the more powerful methods in the standard<br>
library, used in a variety of contexts.</p>
<p>The most basic pattern in which <code>collect()</code> is used is to turn one<br>
collection into another. You take a collection, call [<code>iter</code>] on it,<br>
do a bunch of transformations, and then <code>collect()</code> at the end.</p>
<p><code>collect()</code> can also create instances of types that are not typical<br>
collections. For example, a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/iter/std/string/struct.String.html"><code>String</code></a> can be built from [<code>char</code>]s,<br>
and an iterator of <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/result/enum.Result.html"><code>Result&lt;T, E&gt;</code></a> items can be collected<br>
into <code>Result&lt;Collection&lt;T&gt;, E&gt;</code>. See the examples below for more.</p>
<p>Because <code>collect()</code> is so general, it can cause problems with type<br>
inference. As such, <code>collect()</code> is one of the few times you'll see<br>
the syntax affectionately known as the 'turbofish': <code>::&lt;&gt;</code>. This<br>
helps the inference algorithm understand specifically which collection<br>
you're trying to collect into.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<p>Basic usage:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let a = [1, 2, 3];

let doubled: Vec&lt;i32&gt; = a.iter()
                         .map(|x| x * 2)
                         .collect();

assert_eq!(vec![2, 4, 6], doubled);
</code></pre>
<p>Note that we needed the <code>: Vec&lt;i32&gt;</code> on the left-hand side. This is because<br>
we could collect into, for example, a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/iter/std/collections/struct.VecDeque.html"><code>VecDeque&lt;T&gt;</code></a> instead:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::collections::VecDeque;

let a = [1, 2, 3];

let doubled: VecDeque&lt;i32&gt; = a.iter().map(|x| x * 2).collect();

assert_eq!(2, doubled[0]);
assert_eq!(4, doubled[1]);
assert_eq!(6, doubled[2]);
</code></pre>
<p>Using the 'turbofish' instead of annotating <code>doubled</code>:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let a = [1, 2, 3];

let doubled = a.iter().map(|x| x * 2).collect::&lt;Vec&lt;i32&gt;&gt;();

assert_eq!(vec![2, 4, 6], doubled);
</code></pre>
<p>Because <code>collect()</code> only cares about what you're collecting into, you can<br>
still use a partial type hint, <code>_</code>, with the turbofish:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let a = [1, 2, 3];

let doubled = a.iter().map(|x| x * 2).collect::&lt;Vec&lt;_&gt;&gt;();

assert_eq!(vec![2, 4, 6], doubled);
</code></pre>
<p>Using <code>collect()</code> to make a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/iter/std/string/struct.String.html"><code>String</code></a>:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let chars = ['g', 'd', 'k', 'k', 'n'];

let hello: String = chars.into_iter()
    .map(|x| x as u8)
    .map(|x| (x + 1) as char)
    .collect();

assert_eq!(&quot;hello&quot;, hello);
</code></pre>
<p>If you have a list of <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/result/enum.Result.html"><code>Result&lt;T, E&gt;</code></a>s, you can use <code>collect()</code> to<br>
see if any of them failed:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let results = [Ok(1), Err(&quot;nope&quot;), Ok(3), Err(&quot;bad&quot;)];

let result: Result&lt;Vec&lt;_&gt;, &amp;str&gt; = results.into_iter().collect();

// gives us the first error
assert_eq!(Err(&quot;nope&quot;), result);

let results = [Ok(1), Ok(3)];

let result: Result&lt;Vec&lt;_&gt;, &amp;str&gt; = results.into_iter().collect();

// gives us the list of answers
assert_eq!(Ok(vec![1, 3]), result);
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.method">{`collect`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`::`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`<`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">alloc::vec
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub struct Vec&lt;T, A = Global&gt;
where
    A: Allocator,
{
    buf: RawVec&lt;T, A&gt;,
    len: usize,
}
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>A contiguous growable array type, written as <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code>, short for 'vector'.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut vec = Vec::new();
vec.push(1);
vec.push(2);

assert_eq!(vec.len(), 2);
assert_eq!(vec[0], 1);

assert_eq!(vec.pop(), Some(2));
assert_eq!(vec.len(), 1);

vec[0] = 7;
assert_eq!(vec[0], 7);

vec.extend([1, 2, 3]);

for x in &amp;vec {
    println!(&quot;{x}&quot;);
}
assert_eq!(vec, [7, 1, 2, 3]);
</code></pre>
<p>The <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/macros/macro.vec.html"><code>vec</code></a> macro is provided for convenient initialization:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut vec1 = vec![1, 2, 3];
vec1.push(4);
let vec2 = Vec::from([1, 2, 3, 4]);
assert_eq!(vec1, vec2);
</code></pre>
<p>It can also initialize each element of a <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code> with a given value.<br>
This may be more efficient than performing allocation and initialization<br>
in separate steps, especially when initializing a vector of zeros:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let vec = vec![0; 5];
assert_eq!(vec, [0, 0, 0, 0, 0]);

// The following is equivalent, but potentially slower:
let mut vec = Vec::with_capacity(5);
vec.resize(5, 0);
assert_eq!(vec, [0, 0, 0, 0, 0]);
</code></pre>
<p>For more information, see<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#capacity-and-reallocation">Capacity and Reallocation</a>.</p>
<p>Use a <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code> as an efficient stack:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut stack = Vec::new();

stack.push(1);
stack.push(2);
stack.push(3);

while let Some(top) = stack.pop() {
    // Prints 3, 2, 1
    println!(&quot;{top}&quot;);
}
</code></pre>
<h1 id="indexing">Indexing</h1>
<p>The <code>Vec</code> type allows access to values by index, because it implements the<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/ops/index/trait.Index.html"><code>Index</code></a> trait. An example will be more explicit:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![0, 2, 4, 6];
println!(&quot;{}&quot;, v[1]); // it will display '2'
</code></pre>
<p>However be careful: if you try to access an index which isn't in the <code>Vec</code>,<br>
your software will panic! You cannot do this:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![0, 2, 4, 6];
println!(&quot;{}&quot;, v[6]); // it will panic!
</code></pre>
<p>Use [<code>get</code>] and [<code>get_mut</code>] if you want to check whether the index is in<br>
the <code>Vec</code>.</p>
<h1 id="slicing">Slicing</h1>
<p>A <code>Vec</code> can be mutable. On the other hand, slices are read-only objects.<br>
To get a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/slice/index.html">slice</a>, use <a href="%60&amp;%60"><code>&amp;</code></a>. Example:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">fn read_slice(slice: &amp;[usize]) {
    // ...
}

let v = vec![0, 1];
read_slice(&amp;v);

// ... and that's all!
// you can also do it like this:
let u: &amp;[usize] = &amp;v;
// or like this:
let u: &amp;[_] = &amp;v;
</code></pre>
<p>In Rust, it's more common to pass slices as arguments rather than vectors<br>
when you just want to provide read access. The same goes for [<code>String</code>] and<br>
[<code>&amp;str</code>].</p>
<h1 id="capacity-and-reallocation">Capacity and reallocation</h1>
<p>The capacity of a vector is the amount of space allocated for any future<br>
elements that will be added onto the vector. This is not to be confused with<br>
the <em>length</em> of a vector, which specifies the number of actual elements<br>
within the vector. If a vector's length exceeds its capacity, its capacity<br>
will automatically be increased, but its elements will have to be<br>
reallocated.</p>
<p>For example, a vector with capacity 10 and length 0 would be an empty vector<br>
with space for 10 more elements. Pushing 10 or fewer elements onto the<br>
vector will not change its capacity or cause reallocation to occur. However,<br>
if the vector's length is increased to 11, it will have to reallocate, which<br>
can be slow. For this reason, it is recommended to use <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.with_capacity"><code>Vec::with_capacity</code></a><br>
whenever possible to specify how big the vector is expected to get.</p>
<h1 id="guarantees">Guarantees</h1>
<p>Due to its incredibly fundamental nature, <code>Vec</code> makes a lot of guarantees<br>
about its design. This ensures that it's as low-overhead as possible in<br>
the general case, and can be correctly manipulated in primitive ways<br>
by unsafe code. Note that these guarantees refer to an unqualified <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code>.<br>
If additional type parameters are added (e.g., to support custom allocators),<br>
overriding their defaults may change the behavior.</p>
<p>Most fundamentally, <code>Vec</code> is and always will be a (pointer, capacity, length)<br>
triplet. No more, no less. The order of these fields is completely<br>
unspecified, and you should use the appropriate methods to modify these.<br>
The pointer will never be null, so this type is null-pointer-optimized.</p>
<p>However, the pointer might not actually point to allocated memory. In particular,<br>
if you construct a <code>Vec</code> with capacity 0 via <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.new"><code>Vec::new</code></a>, <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/macros/macro.vec.html"><code>vec![]</code></a>,<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.with_capacity"><code>Vec::with_capacity(0)</code></a>, or by calling [<code>shrink_to_fit</code>]<br>
on an empty Vec, it will not allocate memory. Similarly, if you store zero-sized<br>
types inside a <code>Vec</code>, it will not allocate space for them. <em>Note that in this case<br>
the <code>Vec</code> might not report a [<code>capacity</code>] of 0</em>. <code>Vec</code> will allocate if and only<br>
if <code><br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/mem/fn.size_of.html">size_of::&lt;T&gt;</a>() * <a href="">capacity</a> &gt; 0</code>. In general, <code>Vec</code>'s allocation<br>
details are very subtle --- if you intend to allocate memory using a <code>Vec</code><br>
and use it for something else (either to pass to unsafe code, or to build your<br>
own memory-backed collection), be sure to deallocate this memory by using<br>
<code>from_raw_parts</code> to recover the <code>Vec</code> and then dropping it.</p>
<p>If a <code>Vec</code> <em>has</em> allocated memory, then the memory it points to is on the heap<br>
(as defined by the allocator Rust is configured to use by default), and its<br>
pointer points to [<code>len</code>] initialized, contiguous elements in order (what<br>
you would see if you coerced it to a slice), followed by <code><br>
[capacity] - [len]</code><br>
logically uninitialized, contiguous elements.</p>
<p>A vector containing the elements <code>'a'</code> and <code>'b'</code> with capacity 4 can be<br>
visualized as below. The top part is the <code>Vec</code> struct, it contains a<br>
pointer to the head of the allocation in the heap, length and capacity.<br>
The bottom part is the allocation on the heap, a contiguous memory block.</p>
<pre><code class="language-text">            ptr      len  capacity
       +--------+--------+--------+
       | 0x0123 |      2 |      4 |
       +--------+--------+--------+
            |
            v
Heap   +--------+--------+--------+--------+
       |    'a' |    'b' | uninit | uninit |
       +--------+--------+--------+--------+
</code></pre>
<ul>
<li><strong>uninit</strong> represents memory that is not initialized, see [<code>MaybeUninit</code>].</li>
<li>Note: the ABI is not stable and <code>Vec</code> makes no guarantees about its memory<br>
layout (including the order of fields).</li>
</ul>
<p><code>Vec</code> will never perform a &quot;small optimization&quot; where elements are actually<br>
stored on the stack for two reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>It would make it more difficult for unsafe code to correctly manipulate<br>
a <code>Vec</code>. The contents of a <code>Vec</code> wouldn't have a stable address if it were<br>
only moved, and it would be more difficult to determine if a <code>Vec</code> had<br>
actually allocated memory.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It would penalize the general case, incurring an additional branch<br>
on every access.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><code>Vec</code> will never automatically shrink itself, even if completely empty. This<br>
ensures no unnecessary allocations or deallocations occur. Emptying a <code>Vec</code><br>
and then filling it back up to the same [<code>len</code>] should incur no calls to<br>
the allocator. If you wish to free up unused memory, use<br>
[<code>shrink_to_fit</code>] or [<code>shrink_to</code>].</p>
<p>[<code>push</code>] and [<code>insert</code>] will never (re)allocate if the reported capacity is<br>
sufficient. [<code>push</code>] and [<code>insert</code>] <em>will</em> (re)allocate if<br>
<code><br>
[len] == [capacity]</code>. That is, the reported capacity is completely<br>
accurate, and can be relied on. It can even be used to manually free the memory<br>
allocated by a <code>Vec</code> if desired. Bulk insertion methods <em>may</em> reallocate, even<br>
when not necessary.</p>
<p><code>Vec</code> does not guarantee any particular growth strategy when reallocating<br>
when full, nor when [<code>reserve</code>] is called. The current strategy is basic<br>
and it may prove desirable to use a non-constant growth factor. Whatever<br>
strategy is used will of course guarantee <em>O</em>(1) amortized [<code>push</code>].</p>
<p>It is guaranteed, in order to respect the intentions of the programmer, that<br>
all of <code>vec![e_1, e_2, ..., e_n]</code>, <code>vec![x; n]</code>, and [<code>Vec::with_capacity(n)</code>] produce a <code>Vec</code><br>
that requests an allocation of the exact size needed for precisely <code>n</code> elements from the allocator,<br>
and no other size (such as, for example: a size rounded up to the nearest power of 2).<br>
The allocator will return an allocation that is at least as large as requested, but it may be larger.</p>
<p>It is guaranteed that the [<code>Vec::capacity</code>] method returns a value that is at least the requested capacity<br>
and not more than the allocated capacity.</p>
<p>The method <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/vec/struct.Vec.html#method.shrink_to_fit"><code>Vec::shrink_to_fit</code></a> will attempt to discard excess capacity an allocator has given to a <code>Vec</code>.<br>
If <code><br>
[len] == [capacity]</code>, then a <code>Vec&lt;T&gt;</code> can be converted<br>
to and from a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/boxed/struct.Box.html"><code>Box&lt;[T]&gt;</code></a> without reallocating or moving the elements.<br>
<code>Vec</code> exploits this fact as much as reasonable when implementing common conversions<br>
such as [<code>into_boxed_slice</code>].</p>
<p><code>Vec</code> will not specifically overwrite any data that is removed from it,<br>
but also won't specifically preserve it. Its uninitialized memory is<br>
scratch space that it may use however it wants. It will generally just do<br>
whatever is most efficient or otherwise easy to implement. Do not rely on<br>
removed data to be erased for security purposes. Even if you drop a <code>Vec</code>, its<br>
buffer may simply be reused by another allocation. Even if you zero a <code>Vec</code>'s memory<br>
first, that might not actually happen because the optimizer does not consider<br>
this a side-effect that must be preserved. There is one case which we will<br>
not break, however: using <code>unsafe</code> code to write to the excess capacity,<br>
and then increasing the length to match, is always valid.</p>
<p>Currently, <code>Vec</code> does not guarantee the order in which elements are dropped.<br>
The order has changed in the past and may change again.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type">{`Vec`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`<`}</span><span className="type">{`_`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`>`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`>`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`;`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ tokens`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`.`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::slice
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">impl&lt;T&gt; [T]
pub const fn reverse(&amp;mut self)
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p><code>T</code> = <code>Token</code></p>
<hr>
<p>Reverses the order of elements in the slice, in place.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut v = [1, 2, 3];
v.reverse();
assert!(v == [3, 2, 1]);
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.method">{`reverse`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`;`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">struct Lexer {
    tokens: Vec&lt;Token&gt;,
}
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><a href="#def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_38_7" className="type">{`Lexer`}</a></Tooltip><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`{`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ tokens\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`}`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
\ \ \ \ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`}`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`

\ \ \ \ `}</span><span className="keyword">{`fn`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs::Lexer
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">fn next(&amp;mut self) -&gt; Token
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span id="def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_57_7" className="function">{`next`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="operator">{`&`}</span><span className="keyword">{`mut`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">self: &amp;mut Lexer
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>size = 8, align = 0x8, no Drop</p>

</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>String slices.</p>
<p><em><a href="crate::str">See also the <code>std::str</code> module</a>.</em></p>
<p>The <code>str</code> type, also called a 'string slice', is the most primitive string<br>
type. It is usually seen in its borrowed form, <code>&amp;str</code>. It is also the type<br>
of string literals, <code>&amp;'static str</code>.</p>
<h1 id="basic-usage">Basic Usage</h1>
<p>String literals are string slices:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let hello_world = &quot;Hello, World!&quot;;
</code></pre>
<p>Here we have declared a string slice initialized with a string literal.<br>
String literals have a static lifetime, which means the string <code>hello_world</code><br>
is guaranteed to be valid for the duration of the entire program.<br>
We can explicitly specify <code>hello_world</code>'s lifetime as well:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let hello_world: &amp;'static str = &quot;Hello, world!&quot;;
</code></pre>
<h1 id="representation">Representation</h1>
<p>A <code>&amp;str</code> is made up of two components: a pointer to some bytes, and a<br>
length. You can look at these with the [<code>as_ptr</code>] and [<code>len</code>] methods:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::slice;
use std::str;

let story = &quot;Once upon a time...&quot;;

let ptr = story.as_ptr();
let len = story.len();

// story has nineteen bytes
assert_eq!(19, len);

// We can re-build a str out of ptr and len. This is all unsafe because
// we are responsible for making sure the two components are valid:
let s = unsafe {
    // First, we build a &amp;[u8]...
    let slice = slice::from_raw_parts(ptr, len);

    // ... and then convert that slice into a string slice
    str::from_utf8(slice)
};

assert_eq!(s, Ok(story));
</code></pre>
<p>Note: This example shows the internals of <code>&amp;str</code>. <code>unsafe</code> should not be<br>
used to get a string slice under normal circumstances. Use <code>as_str</code><br>
instead.</p>
<h1 id="invariant">Invariant</h1>
<p>Rust libraries may assume that string slices are always valid UTF-8.</p>
<p>Constructing a non-UTF-8 string slice is not immediate undefined behavior, but any function<br>
called on a string slice may assume that it is valid UTF-8, which means that a non-UTF-8 string<br>
slice can lead to undefined behavior down the road.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type.builtin">{`str`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ ->\ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">rs
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">enum S {
    Atom( /* … */ ),
    Cons( /* … */ ),
}
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><a href="#def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_11_5" className="type">{`S`}</a></Tooltip><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`{`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`

<pre><code class="language-rust">macro_rules! assert_eq // matched arm #0
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Asserts that two expressions are equal to each other (using <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/cmp/trait.PartialEq.html"><code>PartialEq</code></a>).</p>
<p>Assertions are always checked in both debug and release builds, and cannot<br>
be disabled. See [<code>debug_assert_eq!</code>] for assertions that are disabled in<br>
release builds by default.</p>
<p>On panic, this macro will print the values of the expressions with their<br>
debug representations.</p>
<p>Like <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/macros/builtin/macro.assert.html"><code>assert</code></a>, this macro has a second form, where a custom<br>
panic message can be provided.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let a = 3;
let b = 1 + 2;
assert_eq!(a, b);

assert_eq!(a, b, &quot;we are testing addition with {} and {}&quot;, a, b);
</code></pre>

</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">macro_rules! vec // matched arm #2
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Creates a [<code>Vec</code>] containing the arguments.</p>
<p><code>vec!</code> allows <code>Vec</code>s to be defined with the same syntax as array expressions.<br>
There are two forms of this macro:</p>
<ul>
<li>Create a [<code>Vec</code>] containing a given list of elements:</li>
</ul>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![1, 2, 3];
assert_eq!(v[0], 1);
assert_eq!(v[1], 2);
assert_eq!(v[2], 3);
</code></pre>
<ul>
<li>Create a [<code>Vec</code>] from a given element and size:</li>
</ul>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![1; 3];
assert_eq!(v, [1, 1, 1]);
</code></pre>
<p>Note that unlike array expressions this syntax supports all elements<br>
which implement <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/clone/trait.Clone.html"><code>Clone</code></a> and the number of elements doesn't have to be<br>
a constant.</p>
<p>This will use <code>clone</code> to duplicate an expression, so one should be careful<br>
using this with types having a nonstandard <code>Clone</code> implementation. For<br>
example, <code>vec![Rc::new(1); 5]</code> will create a vector of five references<br>
to the same boxed integer value, not five references pointing to independently<br>
boxed integers.</p>
<p>Also, note that <code>vec![expr; 0]</code> is allowed, and produces an empty vector.<br>
This will still evaluate <code>expr</code>, however, and immediately drop the resulting value, so<br>
be mindful of side effects.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.macro">{`vec`}</span></Tooltip><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">alloc::macros

<pre><code class="language-rust">macro_rules! assert_eq // matched arm #0
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Asserts that two expressions are equal to each other (using <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/cmp/trait.PartialEq.html"><code>PartialEq</code></a>).</p>
<p>Assertions are always checked in both debug and release builds, and cannot<br>
be disabled. See [<code>debug_assert_eq!</code>] for assertions that are disabled in<br>
release builds by default.</p>
<p>On panic, this macro will print the values of the expressions with their<br>
debug representations.</p>
<p>Like <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/macros/builtin/macro.assert.html"><code>assert</code></a>, this macro has a second form, where a custom<br>
panic message can be provided.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let a = 3;
let b = 1 + 2;
assert_eq!(a, b);

assert_eq!(a, b, &quot;we are testing addition with {} and {}&quot;, a, b);
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.macro">{`assert_eq`}</span></Tooltip><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::macros

</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">macro_rules! vec // matched arm #2
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Creates a [<code>Vec</code>] containing the arguments.</p>
<p><code>vec!</code> allows <code>Vec</code>s to be defined with the same syntax as array expressions.<br>
There are two forms of this macro:</p>
<ul>
<li>Create a [<code>Vec</code>] containing a given list of elements:</li>
</ul>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![1, 2, 3];
assert_eq!(v[0], 1);
assert_eq!(v[1], 2);
assert_eq!(v[2], 3);
</code></pre>
<ul>
<li>Create a [<code>Vec</code>] from a given element and size:</li>
</ul>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![1; 3];
assert_eq!(v, [1, 1, 1]);
</code></pre>
<p>Note that unlike array expressions this syntax supports all elements<br>
which implement <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/clone/trait.Clone.html"><code>Clone</code></a> and the number of elements doesn't have to be<br>
a constant.</p>
<p>This will use <code>clone</code> to duplicate an expression, so one should be careful<br>
using this with types having a nonstandard <code>Clone</code> implementation. For<br>
example, <code>vec![Rc::new(1); 5]</code> will create a vector of five references<br>
to the same boxed integer value, not five references pointing to independently<br>
boxed integers.</p>
<p>Also, note that <code>vec![expr; 0]</code> is allowed, and produces an empty vector.<br>
This will still evaluate <code>expr</code>, however, and immediately drop the resulting value, so<br>
be mindful of side effects.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.macro">{`vec`}</span></Tooltip><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">alloc::macros

be disabled. See [<code>debug_assert_eq!</code>] for assertions that are disabled in<br>
release builds by default.</p>
<p>On panic, this macro will print the values of the expressions with their<br>
debug representations.</p>
<p>Like <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/macros/builtin/macro.assert.html"><code>assert</code></a>, this macro has a second form, where a custom<br>
panic message can be provided.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let a = 3;
let b = 1 + 2;
assert_eq!(a, b);

assert_eq!(a, b, &quot;we are testing addition with {} and {}&quot;, a, b);
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.macro">{`assert_eq`}</span></Tooltip><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::macros

<pre><code class="language-rust">macro_rules! vec // matched arm #2
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Creates a [<code>Vec</code>] containing the arguments.</p>
<p><code>vec!</code> allows <code>Vec</code>s to be defined with the same syntax as array expressions.<br>
There are two forms of this macro:</p>
<ul>
<li>Create a [<code>Vec</code>] containing a given list of elements:</li>
</ul>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![1, 2, 3];
assert_eq!(v[0], 1);
assert_eq!(v[1], 2);
assert_eq!(v[2], 3);
</code></pre>
<ul>
<li>Create a [<code>Vec</code>] from a given element and size:</li>
</ul>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec![1; 3];
assert_eq!(v, [1, 1, 1]);
</code></pre>
<p>Note that unlike array expressions this syntax supports all elements<br>
which implement <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/clone/trait.Clone.html"><code>Clone</code></a> and the number of elements doesn't have to be<br>
a constant.</p>
<p>This will use <code>clone</code> to duplicate an expression, so one should be careful<br>
using this with types having a nonstandard <code>Clone</code> implementation. For<br>
example, <code>vec![Rc::new(1); 5]</code> will create a vector of five references<br>
to the same boxed integer value, not five references pointing to independently<br>
boxed integers.</p>
<p>Also, note that <code>vec![expr; 0]</code> is allowed, and produces an empty vector.<br>
This will still evaluate <code>expr</code>, however, and immediately drop the resulting value, so<br>
be mindful of side effects.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.macro">{`vec`}</span></Tooltip><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">alloc::macros

<p>size = 4, align = 0x4, no Drop</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span id="def__Users_eric_Documents_Sources_cires_Eric_Song_Nop_github_io_rs_src_main_rs_133_24" className="variable.parameter">{`op`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`:`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">char
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>A character type.</p>
<p>The <code>char</code> type represents a single character. More specifically, since<br>
'character' isn't a well-defined concept in Unicode, <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode<br>
scalar value</a>'.</p>
<p>This documentation describes a number of methods and trait implementations on the<br>
<code>char</code> type. For technical reasons, there is additional, separate<br>
documentation in <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/core/char/index.html">the <code>std::char</code> module</a> as well.</p>
<h1 id="validity-and-layout">Validity and Layout</h1>
<p>A <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode scalar value</a>', which is any '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#code_point">Unicode code point</a>'<br>
other than a <a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#surrogate_code_point">surrogate code point</a>. This has a fixed numerical definition:<br>
code points are in the range 0 to 0x10FFFF, inclusive.<br>
Surrogate code points, used by UTF-16, are in the range 0xD800 to 0xDFFF.</p>
<p>No <code>char</code> may be constructed, whether as a literal or at runtime, that is not a<br>
Unicode scalar value. Violating this rule causes undefined behavior.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Each of these is a compiler error
['\\u{D800}', '\\u{DFFF}', '\\u{110000}'];
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Panics; from_u32 returns None.
char::from_u32(0xDE01).unwrap();
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Undefined behavior
let _ = unsafe { char::from_u32_unchecked(0x110000) };
</code></pre>
<p>Unicode scalar values are also the exact set of values that may be encoded in UTF-8. Because<br>
<code>char</code> values are Unicode scalar values and functions may assume <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/core/primitive.str.html#invariant">incoming <code>str</code> values are<br>
valid UTF-8</a>, it is safe to store any <code>char</code> in a <code>str</code> or read<br>
any character from a <code>str</code> as a <code>char</code>.</p>
<p>The gap in valid <code>char</code> values is understood by the compiler, so in the<br>
below example the two ranges are understood to cover the whole range of<br>
possible <code>char</code> values and there is no error for a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/book/ch06-02-match.html#matches-are-exhaustive">non-exhaustive match</a>.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let c: char = 'a';
match c {
    '\\0' ..= '\\u{D7FF}' =&gt; false,
    '\\u{E000}' ..= '\\u{10FFFF}' =&gt; true,
};
</code></pre>
<p>All Unicode scalar values are valid <code>char</code> values, but not all of them represent a real<br>
character. Many Unicode scalar values are not currently assigned to a character, but may be in<br>
the future (&quot;reserved&quot;); some will never be a character (&quot;noncharacters&quot;); and some may be given<br>
different meanings by different users (&quot;private use&quot;).</p>
<p><code>char</code> is guaranteed to have the same size, alignment, and function call ABI as <code>u32</code> on all<br>
platforms.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::alloc::Layout;
assert_eq!(Layout::new::&lt;char&gt;(), Layout::new::&lt;u32&gt;());
</code></pre>
<h1 id="representation">Representation</h1>
<p><code>char</code> is always four bytes in size. This is a different representation than<br>
a given character would have as part of a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/std/string/struct.String.html"><code>String</code></a>. For example:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec!['h', 'e', 'l', 'l', 'o'];

// five elements times four bytes for each element
assert_eq!(20, v.len() * size_of::&lt;char&gt;());

let s = String::from(&quot;hello&quot;);

// five elements times one byte per element
assert_eq!(5, s.len() * size_of::&lt;u8&gt;());
</code></pre>
<p>As always, remember that a human intuition for 'character' might not map to<br>
Unicode's definitions. For example, despite looking similar, the 'é'<br>
character is one Unicode code point while 'é' is two Unicode code points:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut chars = &quot;é&quot;.chars();
// U+00e9: 'latin small letter e with acute'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{00e9}'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(None, chars.next());

let mut chars = &quot;é&quot;.chars();
// U+0065: 'latin small letter e'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0065}'), chars.next());
// U+0301: 'combining acute accent'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0301}'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(None, chars.next());
</code></pre>
<p>This means that the contents of the first string above <em>will</em> fit into a<br>
<code>char</code> while the contents of the second string <em>will not</em>. Trying to create<br>
a <code>char</code> literal with the contents of the second string gives an error:</p>
<pre><code class="language-text">error: character literal may only contain one codepoint: 'é'
let c = 'é';
        ^^^
</code></pre>
<p>Another implication of the 4-byte fixed size of a <code>char</code> is that<br>
per-<code>char</code> processing can end up using a lot more memory:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let s = String::from(&quot;love: ❤️&quot;);
let v: Vec&lt;char&gt; = s.chars().collect();

assert_eq!(12, size_of_val(&amp;s[..]));
assert_eq!(32, size_of_val(&amp;v[..]));
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type.builtin">{`char`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ ->\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`,`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">u8

</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>A character type.</p>
<p>The <code>char</code> type represents a single character. More specifically, since<br>
'character' isn't a well-defined concept in Unicode, <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode<br>
scalar value</a>'.</p>
<p>This documentation describes a number of methods and trait implementations on the<br>
<code>char</code> type. For technical reasons, there is additional, separate<br>
documentation in <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/core/char/index.html">the <code>std::char</code> module</a> as well.</p>
<h1 id="validity-and-layout">Validity and Layout</h1>
<p>A <code>char</code> is a '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#unicode_scalar_value">Unicode scalar value</a>', which is any '<a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#code_point">Unicode code point</a>'<br>
other than a <a href="https://www.unicode.org/glossary/#surrogate_code_point">surrogate code point</a>. This has a fixed numerical definition:<br>
code points are in the range 0 to 0x10FFFF, inclusive.<br>
Surrogate code points, used by UTF-16, are in the range 0xD800 to 0xDFFF.</p>
<p>No <code>char</code> may be constructed, whether as a literal or at runtime, that is not a<br>
Unicode scalar value. Violating this rule causes undefined behavior.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Each of these is a compiler error
['\\u{D800}', '\\u{DFFF}', '\\u{110000}'];
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Panics; from_u32 returns None.
char::from_u32(0xDE01).unwrap();
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">// Undefined behavior
let _ = unsafe { char::from_u32_unchecked(0x110000) };
</code></pre>
<p>Unicode scalar values are also the exact set of values that may be encoded in UTF-8. Because<br>
<code>char</code> values are Unicode scalar values and functions may assume <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/core/primitive.str.html#invariant">incoming <code>str</code> values are<br>
valid UTF-8</a>, it is safe to store any <code>char</code> in a <code>str</code> or read<br>
any character from a <code>str</code> as a <code>char</code>.</p>
<p>The gap in valid <code>char</code> values is understood by the compiler, so in the<br>
below example the two ranges are understood to cover the whole range of<br>
possible <code>char</code> values and there is no error for a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/book/ch06-02-match.html#matches-are-exhaustive">non-exhaustive match</a>.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let c: char = 'a';
match c {
    '\\0' ..= '\\u{D7FF}' =&gt; false,
    '\\u{E000}' ..= '\\u{10FFFF}' =&gt; true,
};
</code></pre>
<p>All Unicode scalar values are valid <code>char</code> values, but not all of them represent a real<br>
character. Many Unicode scalar values are not currently assigned to a character, but may be in<br>
the future (&quot;reserved&quot;); some will never be a character (&quot;noncharacters&quot;); and some may be given<br>
different meanings by different users (&quot;private use&quot;).</p>
<p><code>char</code> is guaranteed to have the same size, alignment, and function call ABI as <code>u32</code> on all<br>
platforms.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::alloc::Layout;
assert_eq!(Layout::new::&lt;char&gt;(), Layout::new::&lt;u32&gt;());
</code></pre>
<h1 id="representation">Representation</h1>
<p><code>char</code> is always four bytes in size. This is a different representation than<br>
a given character would have as part of a <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/nightly/std/string/struct.String.html"><code>String</code></a>. For example:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec!['h', 'e', 'l', 'l', 'o'];

// five elements times four bytes for each element
assert_eq!(20, v.len() * size_of::&lt;char&gt;());

let s = String::from(&quot;hello&quot;);

// five elements times one byte per element
assert_eq!(5, s.len() * size_of::&lt;u8&gt;());
</code></pre>
<p>As always, remember that a human intuition for 'character' might not map to<br>
Unicode's definitions. For example, despite looking similar, the 'é'<br>
character is one Unicode code point while 'é' is two Unicode code points:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let mut chars = &quot;é&quot;.chars();
// U+00e9: 'latin small letter e with acute'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{00e9}'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(None, chars.next());

let mut chars = &quot;é&quot;.chars();
// U+0065: 'latin small letter e'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0065}'), chars.next());
// U+0301: 'combining acute accent'
assert_eq!(Some('\\u{0301}'), chars.next());
assert_eq!(None, chars.next());
</code></pre>
<p>This means that the contents of the first string above <em>will</em> fit into a<br>
<code>char</code> while the contents of the second string <em>will not</em>. Trying to create<br>
a <code>char</code> literal with the contents of the second string gives an error:</p>
<pre><code class="language-text">error: character literal may only contain one codepoint: 'é'
let c = 'é';
        ^^^
</code></pre>
<p>Another implication of the 4-byte fixed size of a <code>char</code> is that<br>
per-<code>char</code> processing can end up using a lot more memory:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let s = String::from(&quot;love: ❤️&quot;);
let v: Vec&lt;char&gt; = s.chars().collect();

assert_eq!(12, size_of_val(&amp;s[..]));
assert_eq!(32, size_of_val(&amp;v[..]));
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type.builtin">{`char`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ ->\ `}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::option
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub enum Option&lt;T&gt; {
    None,
    Some( /* … */ ),
}
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>The <code>Option</code> type. See <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/option/index.html">the module level documentation</a> for more.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type">{`Option`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`<`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">u8
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>The 8-bit unsigned integer type.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="type.builtin">{`u8`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`,`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`>`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`{`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`

<h1 id="representation">Representation</h1>
<p><code>char</code> is always four bytes in size. This is a different representation than<br>

<pre><code class="language-rust">let v = vec!['h', 'e', 'l', 'l', 'o'];

let s = String::from(&quot;hello&quot;);

// five elements times one byte per element

</code></pre>

<pre><code class="language-rust">macro_rules! assert_eq // matched arm #0
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Asserts that two expressions are equal to each other (using <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/cmp/trait.PartialEq.html"><code>PartialEq</code></a>).</p>
<p>Assertions are always checked in both debug and release builds, and cannot<br>
be disabled. See [<code>debug_assert_eq!</code>] for assertions that are disabled in<br>
release builds by default.</p>

debug representations.</p>

panic message can be provided.</p>

\ \ \ \ `}</span><span className="keyword">{`for`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ line\ `}</span><span className="keyword">{`in`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ std`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`::`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`io`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`::`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">std::io::stdio
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub fn stdin() -&gt; Stdin
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Constructs a new handle to the standard input of the current process.</p>
<p>Each handle returned is a reference to a shared global buffer whose access<br>
is synchronized via a mutex. If you need more explicit control over<br>
locking, see the <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/stdio/struct.Stdin.html#method.lock"><code>Stdin::lock</code></a> method.</p>
<h3 id="note-windows-portability-considerations">Note: Windows Portability Considerations</h3>
<p>When operating in a console, the Windows implementation of this stream does not support<br>
non-UTF-8 byte sequences. Attempting to read bytes that are not valid UTF-8 will return<br>
an error.</p>
<p>In a process with a detached console, such as one using<br>
<code>#![windows_subsystem = &quot;windows&quot;]</code>, or in a child process spawned from such a process,<br>
the contained handle will be null. In such cases, the standard library's <code>Read</code> and<br>
<code>Write</code> will do nothing and silently succeed. All other I/O operations, via the<br>
standard library or via raw Windows API calls, will fail.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<p>Using implicit synchronization:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::io;

fn main() -&gt; io::Result&lt;()&gt; {
    let mut buffer = String::new();
    io::stdin().read_line(&amp;mut buffer)?;
    Ok(())
}
</code></pre>
<p>Using explicit synchronization:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::io::{self, BufRead};

fn main() -&gt; io::Result&lt;()&gt; {
    let mut buffer = String::new();
    let stdin = io::stdin();
    let mut handle = stdin.lock();

    handle.read_line(&amp;mut buffer)?;
    Ok(())
}
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function">{`stdin`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`.`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">std::io::stdio::Stdin
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub fn lock(&amp;self) -&gt; StdinLock&lt;'static&gt;
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Locks this handle to the standard input stream, returning a readable<br>
guard.</p>
<p>The lock is released when the returned lock goes out of scope. The<br>
returned guard also implements the <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/trait.Read.html"><code>Read</code></a> and <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/trait.BufRead.html"><code>BufRead</code></a> traits for<br>
accessing the underlying data.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::io::{self, BufRead};

fn main() -&gt; io::Result&lt;()&gt; {
    let mut buffer = String::new();
    let stdin = io::stdin();
    let mut handle = stdin.lock();

    handle.read_line(&amp;mut buffer)?;
    Ok(())
}
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.method">{`lock`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`.`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">std::io::BufRead
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">pub trait BufRead
pub fn lines(self) -&gt; Lines&lt;Self&gt;
where
    Self: Sized,
    // Bounds from trait:
    Self: Read,
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p><code>Self</code> = <code>StdinLock&lt;'_&gt;</code></p>
<hr>
<p>Returns an iterator over the lines of this reader.</p>
<p>The iterator returned from this function will yield instances of<br>
<code><br>
[io::Result]&lt;<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/string/struct.String.html">String</a>&gt;</code>. Each string returned will <em>not</em> have a newline<br>
byte (the <code>0xA</code> byte) or <code>CRLF</code> (<code>0xD</code>, <code>0xA</code> bytes) at the end.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<p><a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/cursor/struct.Cursor.html"><code>std::io::Cursor</code></a> is a type that implements <code>BufRead</code>. In<br>
this example, we use <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/cursor/struct.Cursor.html"><code>Cursor</code></a> to iterate over all the lines in a byte<br>
slice.</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::io::{self, BufRead};

let cursor = io::Cursor::new(b&quot;lorem\\nipsum\\r\\ndolor&quot;);

let mut lines_iter = cursor.lines().map(|l| l.unwrap());
assert_eq!(lines_iter.next(), Some(String::from(&quot;lorem&quot;)));
assert_eq!(lines_iter.next(), Some(String::from(&quot;ipsum&quot;)));
assert_eq!(lines_iter.next(), Some(String::from(&quot;dolor&quot;)));
assert_eq!(lines_iter.next(), None);
</code></pre>
<h1 id="errors">Errors</h1>
<p>Each line of the iterator has the same error semantics as <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/trait.BufRead.html#method.read_line"><code>BufRead::read_line</code></a>.</p>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.method">{`lines`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ `}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`{`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`
\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ `}</span><span className="keyword">{`let`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`\ line\ =\ line`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`.`}</span><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">core::result::Result
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">impl&lt;T, E&gt; Result&lt;T, E&gt;
pub fn unwrap(self) -&gt; T
where
    E: fmt::Debug,
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p><code>T</code> = <code>String</code>, <code>E</code> = <code>Error</code></p>
<hr>
<p>Returns the contained <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/result/enum.Result.html#variant.Ok"><code>Ok</code></a> value, consuming the <code>self</code> value.</p>
<p>Because this function may panic, its use is generally discouraged.<br>
Panics are meant for unrecoverable errors, and<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/book/ch09-01-unrecoverable-errors-with-panic.html">may abort the entire program</a>.</p>
<p>Instead, prefer to use <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/book/ch09-02-recoverable-errors-with-result.html#a-shortcut-for-propagating-errors-the--operator">the <code>?</code> (try) operator</a>, or pattern matching<br>
to handle the <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/result/enum.Result.html#variant.Err"><code>Err</code></a> case explicitly, or call [<code>unwrap_or</code>],<br>
[<code>unwrap_or_else</code>], or [<code>unwrap_or_default</code>].</p>
<h1 id="panics">Panics</h1>
<p>Panics if the value is an <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/result/enum.Result.html#variant.Err"><code>Err</code></a>, with a panic message provided by the<br>
<a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/core/result/enum.Result.html#variant.Err"><code>Err</code></a>'s value.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<p>Basic usage:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let x: Result&lt;u32, &amp;str&gt; = Ok(2);
assert_eq!(x.unwrap(), 2);
</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">let x: Result&lt;u32, &amp;str&gt; = Err(&quot;emergency failure&quot;);
x.unwrap(); // panics with \`emergency failure\`
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.method">{`unwrap`}</span></Tooltip><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`(`}</span><span className="punctuation.bracket">{`)`}</span><span className="punctuation.delimiter">{`;`}</span><span className="cire_text">{`

</code></pre>

</code></pre>
<pre><code class="language-rust">macro_rules! println // matched arm #1
</code></pre>
<hr>
<p>Prints to the standard output, with a newline.</p>
<p>On all platforms, the newline is the LINE FEED character (<code>\\n</code>/<code>U+000A</code>) alone<br>
(no additional CARRIAGE RETURN (<code>\\r</code>/<code>U+000D</code>)).</p>
<p>This macro uses the same syntax as <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/macros/macro.format.html"><code>format</code></a>, but writes to the standard output instead.<br>
See [<code>std::fmt</code>] for more information.</p>
<p>The <code>println!</code> macro will lock the standard output on each call. If you call<br>
<code>println!</code> within a hot loop, this behavior may be the bottleneck of the loop.<br>
To avoid this, lock stdout with <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/std/io/stdio/struct.Stdout.html"><code>io::stdout().lock()</code></a>:</p>
<pre><code class="language-rust">use std::io::{stdout, Write};

let mut lock = stdout().lock();
writeln!(lock, &quot;hello world&quot;).unwrap();
</code></pre>
<p>Use <code>println!</code> only for the primary output of your program. Use<br>
[<code>eprintln!</code>] instead to print error and progress messages.</p>
<p>See the formatting documentation in <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/fmt/index.html"><code>std::fmt</code></a><br>
for details of the macro argument syntax.</p>
<h1 id="panics">Panics</h1>
<p>Panics if writing to [<code>io::stdout</code>] fails.</p>
<p>Writing to non-blocking stdout can cause an error, which will lead<br>
this macro to panic.</p>
<h1 id="examples">Examples</h1>
<pre><code class="language-rust">println!(); // prints just a newline
println!(&quot;hello there!&quot;);
println!(&quot;format {} arguments&quot;, &quot;some&quot;);
let local_variable = &quot;some&quot;;
println!(&quot;format {local_variable} arguments&quot;);
</code></pre>
` }} />} placement="top" trigger={['hover']}><span className="function.macro">{`println`}</span></Tooltip><Tooltip overlay={<div className="cire-markdown" dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: `<pre><code class="language-rust">std::macros
</code></pre>

</code></pre>

<p>This macro uses the same syntax as <a href="https://doc.rust-lang.org/stable/alloc/macros/macro.format.html"><code>format</code></a>, but writes to the standard output instead.<br>
See [<code>std::fmt</code>] for more information.</p>
